WN0IjtmdW5jdGlvbiByKHQsbil7dHJ5e09iamVjdC5kZWZpbmVQcm9wZXJ0eSh3aW5kb3csdCx7dmFsdWU6bix3cml0YWJsZTohMX0pfWNhdGNoKGUpe28ud2FybigiQ2Fubm90IGRlZmluZSByZWFkLW9ubHkgcHJvcGVydHkgb2Ygd2luZG93IG9iamVjdDogIitlKX19dmFyIG89dCg0KS5sb2dnZXIsaT1mdW5jdGlvbih0KXsibG9hZGluZyIhPT1kb2N1bWVudC5yZWFkeVN0YXRlP3QoKTpkb2N1bWVudC5hZGRFdmVudExpc3RlbmVyKCJET01Db250ZW50TG9hZGVkIix0KX07bi5leHBvcnRzPXtkZWZpbmVSZWFkT25seVByb3BlcnR5OnIscmVhZHk6aX19LHs0OjR9XSw3OltmdW5jdGlvbih0LG4sZSl7ZnVuY3Rpb24gcih0LG4pe2NvbnN0IGU9YVtuXTt2b2lkIDAhPT1lJiYoZT4wJiYodC5hc3NlcnQ9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjUmJih0LnRyYWNlPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSksZT4xMCYmKHQuZGVidWc9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjIwJiYodC5sb2c9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjMwJiYodC5pbmZvPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSksZT40MCYmKHQud2Fybj1mdW5jdGlvbigpe30pLGU+NTAmJih0LmVycm9yPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSkpfWZ1bmN0aW9uIG8odCxuKXtuPW58fHt9O2NvbnN0IGU9bi5sZXZlbHx8ImRlYnVnIjtyKHQsZSl9ZnVuY3Rpb24gaSh0KXtjb25zdCBuPU9iamVjdC5jcmVhdGUoY29uc29sZSk7cmV0dXJuIG8obix0KSxufWNvbnN0IGE9e2FsbDowLHRyYWNlOjUsZGVidWc6MTAsbG9nOjIwLGluZm86MzAsd2Fybjo0MCxlcnJvcjo1MH0scz1pKCk7bi5leHBvcnRzPXtsb2dnZXI6cyxjb25maWd1cmU6byxjcmVhdGU6aX19LHt9XX0se30sWzFdKTs="));</script>		<div data-icon="close"></em>    </div><div id="content" class="md-content">            <div class="header-ad">                    									<div style="width: 728px; height: 90px" id="div-gpt-ad-769b7270-29f7-4350-9d24-938ae8558e1e" data-slot="TOP" class="google-ad-iframe ui-hide-on-modal " adonis-marker>			<script>googletag.cmd.push( function() { headertag.display( 'div-gpt-ad-769b7270-29f7-4350-9d24-938ae8558e1e' ); } );</script>		</div>	</div>            <main class="md-article-container container template-large_paginated">            <h1>Diplomacy</h1><div class="md-byline">	<div class="written-by">			<span>Written By: </span>			<ul>				<li><a href="/contributor/Chas-W-Freeman/5587">Chas. W. Freeman</a></li>				<li><a href="/contributor/Sally-Marks/3736">Sally Marks</a></li>				</ul>			</div>	<div class="last-updated hidden-xs">			<a data-type="accordion-link" href="#accordion-article-history" rel="nofollow">See Article History</a>			</div>	</div><div class="grid">				<div class="grid-sm">					<article id="article-content" class="content"><section id="ref1" data-level="1"><p><strong><span id="ref321580"></span>Diplomacy</strong>,  the established method of influencing the decisions and behaviour of foreign <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/government" class="md-crosslink">governments</a> and peoples through <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/dialogue" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="dialogue">dialogue</a>, <span id="ref795996"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/negotiation" class="md-crosslink">negotiation</a>, and other measures short of war or violence. Modern diplomatic practices are a product of the post-<a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Renaissance" class="md-crosslink">Renaissance</a> European state system. Historically, diplomacy meant the conduct of official (usually bilateral) relations between <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/sovereign" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="sovereign">sovereign</a> states. By the 20th century, however, the diplomatic practices pioneered in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a> had been adopted throughout the world, and diplomacy had expanded to cover summit meetings and other international conferences, parliamentary diplomacy, the international activities of supranational and subnational entities, unofficial diplomacy by nongovernmental elements, and the work of international civil servants.</p><!--[P1]--><p>The term <em>diplomacy</em> is derived via French from the ancient Greek <em>diplōma</em>, composed of <em>diplo</em>, meaning “folded in two,” and the suffix <em>-ma</em>, meaning “an object.” The folded document conferred a privilege—often a permit to travel—on the bearer, and the term came to denote documents through which princes granted such favours. Later it applied to all solemn documents issued by chancelleries, especially those containing agreements between <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/sovereigns" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="sovereigns">sovereigns</a>. Diplomacy later became identified with <span id="ref82999"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-relations" class="md-crosslink">international relations</a>, and the direct tie to documents lapsed (except in <span id="ref83000"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/diplomatics" class="md-crosslink">diplomatics</a>, which is the science of authenticating old official documents). In the 18th century the French term <em>diplomate</em> (“diplomat” or “diplomatist”) came to refer to a person authorized to negotiate on behalf of a state.</p><!--[P2]--><p>This article discusses the nature of diplomacy, its history, and the ways in which modern diplomacy is conducted, including the selection and training of diplomats and the organization of diplomatic bodies. For a discussion of the legal rules governing diplomatic negotiation and the preparation of treaties and other agreements, <em>see</em> <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-law" class="md-crosslink">international law</a>. One <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/venue" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="venue">venue</a> for diplomacy, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/United-Nations" class="md-crosslink">United Nations</a> (UN), is considered in detail under that title.</p><!--[P3]--></section><div class="major-reference-upsell">			<iframe src="/GPTIframe/ajax/sync/EB_HOUSE_DESKTOP" scrolling="no" width="640" height="106"></iframe>		</div><section id="ref233733" data-level="1"><h2 class="h1">Nature and purpose</h2><p>Diplomacy is often confused with <span id="ref83001"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/foreign-policy" class="md-crosslink">foreign policy</a>, but the terms are not synonymous. Diplomacy is the chief, but not the only, instrument of foreign policy, which is set by political leaders, though diplomats (in addition to military and intelligence officers) may advise them. Foreign policy establishes goals, prescribes strategies, and sets the broad <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/tactics" class="md-crosslink autoxref">tactics</a> to be used in their accomplishment. It may employ secret agents, subversion, war, or other forms of violence as well as diplomacy to achieve its objectives. Diplomacy is the principal substitute for the use of force or underhanded means in statecraft; it is how <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/comprehensive" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="comprehensive">comprehensive</a> national power is applied to the peaceful adjustment of differences between <span id="ref83002"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/state-sovereign-political-entity" class="md-crosslink">states</a>. It may be coercive (i.e., backed by the threat to apply punitive measures or to use force) but is overtly nonviolent. Its primary tools are international dialogue and negotiation, primarily conducted by accredited <span id="ref796040"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/envoy" class="md-crosslink">envoys</a> (a term derived from the French <em>envoyé</em>, meaning “one who is sent”) and other political leaders. Unlike foreign policy, which generally is enunciated publicly, most diplomacy is conducted in confidence, though both the fact that it is in progress and its results are almost always made public in contemporary <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/20th-century-international-relations-2085155" class="md-crosslink">international relations</a>.</p><!--[P4]--><p>The purpose of foreign policy is to further a state’s interests, which are derived from <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/geography" class="md-crosslink">geography</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/history" class="md-crosslink">history</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/economics" class="md-crosslink">economics</a>, and the distribution of international power. Safeguarding national independence, security, and integrity—territorial, political, economic, and moral—is viewed as a country’s primary obligation, followed by preserving a wide freedom of action for the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/state-sovereign-political-entity" class="md-crosslink">state</a>. The political leaders, traditionally of sovereign states, who devise foreign policy pursue what they perceive to be the national interest, adjusting national policies to changes in external conditions and technology. Primary responsibility for supervising the execution of policy may lie with the head of state or government, a cabinet or a nominally nongovernmental <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/collective" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="collective">collective</a> leadership, the staff of the country’s leader, or a <span id="ref83003"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/foreign-minister" class="md-crosslink">minister</a> who presides over the foreign ministry, directs policy execution, supervises the ministry’s officials, and instructs the country’s diplomats abroad.</p><!--[P5]--><p>The purpose of diplomacy is to strengthen the state, nation, or organization it serves in relation to others by advancing the interests in its charge. To this end, diplomatic activity endeavours to maximize a group’s advantages without the risk and expense of using force and preferably without causing resentment. It habitually, but not invariably, strives to preserve peace; diplomacy is strongly inclined toward negotiation to achieve agreements and resolve issues between states. Even in times of peace, diplomacy may involve coercive threats of economic or other punitive measures or demonstrations of the capability to impose unilateral solutions to disputes by the application of military power. However, diplomacy normally seeks to develop goodwill toward the state it represents, nurturing relations with foreign states and peoples that will ensure their cooperation or—failing that—their neutrality.</p><!--[P6]--><p>When diplomacy fails, <span id="ref796041"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/war" class="md-crosslink">war</a> may ensue; however, diplomacy is useful even during war. It conducts the passages from protest to menace, dialogue to negotiation, ultimatum to reprisal, and war to peace and reconciliation with other states. Diplomacy builds and tends the coalitions that deter or make war. It disrupts the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/alliance-politics" class="md-crosslink">alliances</a> of enemies and sustains the passivity of potentially hostile powers. It contrives war’s termination, and it forms, strengthens, and sustains the peace that follows conflict. Over the long term, diplomacy strives to build an international order <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/conducive" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="conducive">conducive</a> to the nonviolent resolution of disputes and expanded cooperation between states.</p><!--[P7]--><p>Diplomats are the primary—but far from the only—practitioners of diplomacy. They are specialists in carrying messages and negotiating adjustments in relations and the resolution of quarrels between states and peoples. Their weapons are words, backed by the power of the state or organization they represent. Diplomats help leaders to understand the attitudes and actions of foreigners and to develop strategies and tactics that will shape the behaviour of foreigners, especially foreign governments. The wise use of diplomats is a key to successful foreign policy.</p><!--[P8]--></section><!--[H3]--><section id="ref59476" data-level="1"><h2 class="h1">History of diplomacy</h2><section id="ref59477" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The <span id="ref83004"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/primitive-culture" class="md-crosslink">ancient world</a></h2><p>The view in late <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/medieval" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="medieval">medieval</a> Europe that the first diplomats were angels, or messengers from heaven to earth, is perhaps fanciful, but some elements of diplomacy predate recorded history. Early societies had some attributes of states, and the first <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-law" class="md-crosslink">international law</a> arose from intertribal relations. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/tribe-anthropology" class="md-crosslink">Tribes</a> negotiated marriages and regulations on trade and hunting. Messengers and envoys were accredited, sacred, and inviolable; they usually carried some emblem, such as a message stick, and were received with elaborate ceremonies. <span id="ref796042"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/women" class="md-crosslink">Women</a> often were used as envoys because of their perceived mysterious sanctity and their use of “sexual wiles”; it is believed that women regularly were entrusted with the vitally important task of negotiating peace in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/primitive-culture" class="md-crosslink">primitive cultures</a>.</p><!--[P9]--><p>Information regarding the diplomacy of early peoples is based on sparse evidence. There are traces of Egyptian diplomacy dating to the 14th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>, but none has been found in western <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Africa" class="md-crosslink">Africa</a> before the 9th century <span class="text-smallcaps">ce</span>. The inscriptions on the walls of abandoned Mayan cities indicate that exchanges of envoys were frequent, though almost nothing is known of the substance or style of Mayan and other pre-Columbian Central American diplomacy. In <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/South-America" class="md-crosslink">South America</a> the dispatch of envoys by the expanding Inca empire appears to have been a prelude to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/conquest-international-law" class="md-crosslink autoxref">conquest</a> rather than an exercise in bargaining between sovereigns.</p><!--[P10]--><p>The greatest knowledge of early diplomacy comes from the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Middle-East" class="md-crosslink">Middle East</a>, the Mediterranean, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">China</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a>. Records of treaties between Mesopotamian city-states date from about 2850 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. Thereafter, <span id="ref83005"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Akkadian-language" class="md-crosslink">Akkadian</a> (Babylonian) became the first diplomatic language, serving as the international tongue of the Middle East until it was replaced by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Aramaic-language" class="md-crosslink">Aramaic</a>. A diplomatic <span id="ref83006"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Amarna-Letters" class="md-crosslink">correspondence</a> from the 14th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span> existed between the <span id="ref83007"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/ancient-Egypt" class="md-crosslink">Egyptian</a> court and a <span id="ref83008"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hittite" class="md-crosslink">Hittite</a> king on cuneiform tablets in Akkadian—the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/language" class="md-crosslink">language</a> of neither. The oldest <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/treaty" class="md-crosslink">treaties</a> of which full texts survive, from about 1280 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>, were between <span id="ref83009"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Ramses-II-king-of-Egypt" class="md-crosslink">Ramses II</a> of Egypt and Hittite leaders. There is significant evidence of Assyrian diplomacy in the 7th century and, chiefly in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Bible" class="md-crosslink">Bible</a>, of the relations of Jewish <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/tribe-anthropology" class="md-crosslink">tribes</a> with each other and other peoples.</p><!--[P11]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="527"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/65/3865-004-18AACBAA.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/65/3865-004-18AACBAA.jpg" alt="Ramses II" data-width="267" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Ramses II</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">A larger-than-life Ramses II towering over his prisoners and clutching them by the hair. Limestone bas-relief from Memphis, Egypt, 1290–24 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>; in the Egyptian Museum, Cairo.</span><cite>Egyptian Museum, Cairo; photograph, O. Louis Mazzatenta/National Geographic Image Collection</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML11]--><span id="ref59477" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233734" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref796043"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/history-of-China" class="md-crosslink">China</a></h2><p>The first records of Chinese and Indian diplomacy date from the 1st millennium <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. By the 8th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span> the Chinese had leagues, missions, and an organized system of polite discourse between their many “<span id="ref796044"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Warring-States" class="md-crosslink">warring states</a>,” including resident envoys who served as hostages to the good behaviour of those who sent them. The sophistication of this tradition, which emphasized the practical virtues of <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/ethical" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="ethical">ethical</a> behaviour in relations between states (no doubt in reaction to actual amorality), is well documented in the Chinese classics. Its essence is perhaps best captured by the advice of <span id="ref796045"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Zhuangzi" class="md-crosslink">Zhuangzi</a> to “diplomats” at the beginning of the 3rd century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. He advised them that</p><!--[P12]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="9429"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/03/12903-004-233EA498.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/03/12903-004-233EA498.jpg" alt="Zhuangzi, detail, ink on silk; in the National Palace Museum, Taipei, Taiwan." data-width="150" data-height="328"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Zhuangzi, detail, ink on silk; in the National Palace Museum, Taipei, Taiwan.</span><cite>Courtesy of the National Palace Museum, Taiwan, Republic of China</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML12]--><blockquote><p>if relations between states are close, they may establish mutual trust through daily interaction; but if relations are distant, mutual confidence can only be established by exchanges of messages. Messages must be conveyed by messengers [diplomats]. Their contents may be either pleasing to both sides or likely to engender anger between them. Faithfully conveying such messages is the most difficult task under the heavens, for if the words are such as to evoke a positive response on both sides, there will be the temptation to exaggerate them with flattery and, if they are unpleasant, there will be a tendency to make them even more biting. In either case, the truth will be lost. If truth is lost, mutual trust will also be lost. If mutual trust is lost, the messenger himself may be imperiled. Therefore, I say to you that it is a wise rule: “always to speak the truth and never to embellish it. In this way, you will avoid much harm to yourselves.”</p></blockquote><p>This tradition of equal diplomatic dealings between contending states within <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">China</a> was ended by the country’s unification under the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/art/qin-musical-instrument" class="md-crosslink">Qin</a> emperor in 221 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span> and the consolidation of unity under the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Han-dynasty" class="md-crosslink">Han dynasty</a> in 206 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. Under the Han and succeeding <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/dynasties" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="dynasties">dynasties</a>, China emerged as the largest, most populous, technologically most-advanced, and best-governed society in the world. The arguments of earlier Chinese philosophers, such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Mencius-Chinese-philosopher" class="md-crosslink">Mencius</a>, prevailed; the best way for a state to exercise influence abroad, they had said, was to develop a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/moral" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="moral">moral</a> society worthy of emulation by admiring foreigners and to wait confidently for them to come to China to learn.</p><!--[P14]--><p>Once each succeeding Chinese <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/dynasty" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="dynasty">dynasty</a> had consolidated its rule at home and established its borders with the non-Chinese world, its foreign relations with the outside world were typically limited to the defense of China’s borders against foreign attacks or incursions, the reception of emissaries from neighbouring states seeking to ingratiate themselves and to trade with the Chinese state, and the control of foreign merchants in specific ports designated for <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-trade" class="md-crosslink autoxref">foreign trade</a>. With rare exceptions (e.g., official missions to study and collect Buddhist scriptures in India in the 5th and 7th centuries and the famous voyages of discovery of the Ming admiral <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Zheng-He" class="md-crosslink">Zheng He</a> in the early 15th century), Chinese leaders and diplomats waited at home for foreigners to pay their respects rather than venturing abroad themselves. This “<span id="ref796046"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/tributary-system" class="md-crosslink">tributary system</a>” lasted until European <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/colonialism" class="md-crosslink">colonialism</a> overwhelmed it and introduced to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Asia" class="md-crosslink">Asia</a> the European concepts of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sovereignty" class="md-crosslink">sovereignty</a>, suzerainty, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sphere-of-influence" class="md-crosslink">spheres of influence</a>, and other diplomatic norms, traditions, and practices.</p><!--[P15]--></section><span id="ref59477" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233735" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref796047"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/history-of-India" class="md-crosslink">India</a></h2><p>Ancient <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a> was home to an equally sophisticated but very different diplomatic tradition. This tradition was systematized and described in the <span id="ref796048"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Artha-shastra" class="md-crosslink">Artha-shastra</a> (one of the oldest books in <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/secular" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="secular">secular</a> Sanskrit literature) by <span id="ref796049"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Kautilya" class="md-crosslink">Kautilya</a>, a clever and reputedly unscrupulous scholar-statesman who helped the young <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Chandragupta" class="md-crosslink">Chandragupta</a> to overthrow Macedonian rule in northern India and to establish the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Mauryan-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Mauryan dynasty</a> at the end of the 4th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. The ruthlessly realistic state system codified in the Artha-shastra insisted that foreign relations be determined by self-interest rather than by ethical considerations. It graded state power with respect to five factors and emphasized <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/espionage" class="md-crosslink autoxref">espionage</a>, diplomatic maneuver, and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/contention" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="contention">contention</a> by 12 categories of states within a complex geopolitical matrix. It also posited four expedients of statecraft (conciliation, seduction, subversion, and coercion) and six forms of state policy (peace, war, nonalignment, alliances, shows of force, and double-dealing). To execute policies derived from these strategic geometries, ancient India fielded three categories of diplomats (<span id="ref83033"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/plenipotentiary" class="md-crosslink">plenipotentiaries</a>, envoys entrusted with a single issue or mission, and royal messengers); a type of consular agent (similar to the Greek <em>proxenos</em>), who was charged with managing commercial relations and transactions; and two kinds of spies (those charged with the collection of intelligence and those entrusted with subversion and other forms of covert action).</p><!--[P16]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="231263"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/13/171213-004-7BC0372A.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/13/171213-004-7BC0372A.jpg" alt="Kautilya" data-width="338" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Kautilya</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Statue of the Indian statesman and philosopher Kautilya, Nagpur, Maharashtra, India. </span><cite>Vence1234</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML16]--><p>Detailed rules regulated diplomatic immunities and privileges, the inauguration and termination of diplomatic missions, and the selection and duties of envoys. Thus, Kautilya describes the “duties of an envoy” as “sending information to his king, ensuring maintenance of the terms of a treaty, upholding his king’s honour, acquiring allies, instigating dissension among the friends of his enemy, conveying secret agents and troops [into enemy territory], suborning the kinsmen of the enemy to his own king’s side, acquiring clandestinely gems and other valuable material for his own king, <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/ascertaining" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="ascertaining">ascertaining</a> secret information and showing valour in liberating hostages [held by the enemy].” He further stipulates that no envoys should ever be harmed, and, even if they deliver an “unpleasant” message, they should not be detained.</p><!--[P17]--><p>The region within which this system operated was separated from its neighbours by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/desert" class="md-crosslink">deserts</a>, seas, and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Himalayas" class="md-crosslink">Himalayas</a>. India had very little political connection to the affairs of other regions of the world until <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Alexander-the-Great" class="md-crosslink">Alexander the Great</a> conquered its northern regions in 326 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. The subsequent establishment of the native <span id="ref796050"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Mauryan-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Mauryan empire</a> ushered in a new era in Indian diplomatic history that was marked by efforts to extend both Indian religious doctrines (i.e., <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Buddhism" class="md-crosslink">Buddhism</a>) and political influence beyond South Asia. The Mauryan <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/emperor-title" class="md-crosslink">emperor</a> <span id="ref796051"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Ashoka" class="md-crosslink">Ashoka</a> was particularly active, receiving several emissaries from the Macedonian-ruled kingdoms and dispatching numerous <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Brahman-caste" class="md-crosslink">Brahman</a>-led missions of his own to West, Central, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Southeast-Asia" class="md-crosslink">Southeast Asia</a>. Such contacts continued for centuries until the ascendancy of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Rajput" class="md-crosslink">Rajput</a> kingdoms (8th to 13th century <span class="text-smallcaps">ce</span>) again isolated northern India from the rest of the world. Outside the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Chola-dynasty" class="md-crosslink">Chola dynasty</a> and other Dravidian kingdoms of South India, which continued diplomatic and cultural exchanges with Southeast Asia and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">China</a> and preserved the text and memories of the Artha-shastra, India’s distinctive mode of diplomatic reasoning and early traditions were forgotten and replaced by those of its Muslim and British conquerors.</p><!--[P18]--></section><span id="ref59477" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233736" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">Greece</h2><p>The tradition that ultimately inspired the birth of modern diplomacy in post-Renaissance <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a> and that led to the present world system of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/20th-century-international-relations-2085155" class="md-crosslink">international relations</a> began in <span id="ref83010"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/ancient-Greece" class="md-crosslink">ancient Greece</a>. The earliest evidence of Greek diplomacy can be found in its <a href="https://www.britannica.com/art/literature" class="md-crosslink">literature</a>, notably in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Homer-Greek-poet" class="md-crosslink">Homer</a>’s <em>Iliad</em> and <em>Odyssey</em>. Otherwise, the first traces of interstate relations concern the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/sports/Olympic-Games" class="md-crosslink">Olympic Games</a> of 776 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>. In the 6th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span> the <span id="ref83011"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/amphictyony" class="md-crosslink">amphictyonic</a> leagues maintained interstate assemblies with extraterritorial rights and permanent secretariats. <span id="ref83012"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Sparta" class="md-crosslink">Sparta</a> was actively forming alliances in the mid-6th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>, and by 500 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span> it had created the <span id="ref796052"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Peloponnesian-League" class="md-crosslink">Peloponnesian League</a>. In the 5th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>, <span id="ref83013"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Athens" class="md-crosslink">Athens</a> led the <span id="ref83014"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Delian-League" class="md-crosslink">Delian League</a> during the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Greco-Persian-Wars" class="md-crosslink">Greco-Persian Wars</a>.</p><!--[P19]--><p>Greek diplomacy took many forms. <span id="ref83015"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/art/herald-medieval-officer" class="md-crosslink">Heralds</a>, references to whom can be found in prehistory, were the first diplomats and were protected by the <span id="ref83017"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Greek-mythology" class="md-crosslink">gods</a> with an <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/immunity-law" class="md-crosslink">immunity</a> that other envoys lacked. Their protector was <span id="ref83016"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Hermes-Greek-mythology" class="md-crosslink">Hermes</a>, the messenger of the gods, who became associated with all diplomacy. The herald of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Zeus" class="md-crosslink">Zeus</a>, Hermes was noted for persuasiveness and eloquence but also for knavery, shiftiness, and dishonesty, imparting to diplomacy a reputation that its practitioners still try to live down.</p><!--[P20]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="36471"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/00/43900-004-D12496BC.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/00/43900-004-D12496BC.jpg" alt="Hermes" data-width="211" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Hermes</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Hermes bearing a caduceus, Roman copy, <em>c.</em> 1st century <span class="text-smallcaps">ce</span>, of a Greek sculpture, <em>c.</em> 350 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>; in the British Museum.</span><cite>Courtesy of the trustees of the British Museum</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML20]--><p>Because heralds were inviolable, they were the favoured channels of contact in wartime. They preceded <span id="ref83018"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/envoy" class="md-crosslink">envoys</a> to arrange for safe passage. Whereas heralds traveled alone, envoys journeyed in small groups, to ensure each other’s loyalty. They usually were at least 50 years old and were politically prominent figures. Because they were expected to sway foreign assemblies, envoys were chosen for their oratorical skills. Although such missions were frequent, Greek diplomacy was episodic rather than continuous. Unlike modern <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ambassador" class="md-crosslink">ambassadors</a>, heralds and envoys were short-term visitors in the city-states whose policies they sought to influence.</p><!--[P21]--><p>In marked contrast to diplomatic relations, commercial and other apolitical relations between city-states were conducted on a continuous basis. Greek consular agents, or <em><span id="ref83019"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/proxenos" class="md-crosslink">proxeni</a></em>, were citizens of the city in which they resided, not of the city-state that employed them. Like envoys, they had a secondary task of gathering information, but their primary responsibility was trade. Although <em>proxeni</em> initially represented one Greek city-state in another, eventually they became far-flung; in his famed work <em>History</em>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Herodotus-Greek-historian" class="md-crosslink">Herodotus</a> indicates that there were Greek consuls in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Egypt" class="md-crosslink">Egypt</a> in about 550 <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>.</p><!--[P22]--><p>The Greeks developed archives, a diplomatic vocabulary, principles of international conduct that anticipated international law, and many other elements of modern diplomacy. Their envoys and entourages enjoyed <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/diplomatic-immunity" class="md-crosslink">diplomatic immunity</a> for their official correspondence and personal <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/property-legal-concept" class="md-crosslink">property</a>. Truces, neutrality, commercial conventions, conferences, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/treaty" class="md-crosslink">treaties</a>, and alliances were common. In one 25-year period of the 4th century <span class="text-smallcaps">bce</span>, for example, there were eight Greco-Persian congresses, where even the smallest states had the right to be heard.</p><!--[P23]--></section><span id="ref59477" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233737" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref83021"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/ancient-Rome" class="md-crosslink">Rome</a></h2><p><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Rome" class="md-crosslink">Rome</a> inherited what the Greeks devised and adapted it to the task of imperial administration. As Rome expanded, it often negotiated with representatives of conquered areas, to which it granted partial self-<a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/government" class="md-crosslink">government</a> by way of a treaty. Treaties were made with other states under Greek international law. During the Roman Republic the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Senate-Roman-history" class="md-crosslink">Senate</a> conducted foreign policy, though a department for foreign affairs was established. Later, under the Empire, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/emperor-title" class="md-crosslink">emperor</a> was the ultimate decision maker in foreign affairs. Envoys were received with ceremony and magnificence, and they and their aides were granted immunity.</p><!--[P24]--><p>Roman envoys were sent abroad with written instructions from their government. Sometimes a messenger, or <em><span id="ref83022"></span>nuntius</em>, was sent, usually to towns. For larger responsibilities a <em><span id="ref83023"></span>legatio</em> (embassy) of 10 or 12 <em>legati</em> (ambassadors) was organized under a president. The <em>legati</em>, who were leading citizens chosen for their skill at oratory, were inviolable. Rome also created sophisticated <span id="ref83024"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/archives" class="md-crosslink">archives</a>, which were staffed by trained archivists. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/paleography" class="md-crosslink">Paleographic</a> techniques were developed to decipher and authenticate ancient documents. Other archivists specialized in diplomatic precedents and procedures, which became formalized. For centuries these archive-based activities were the major preoccupation of diplomacy in and around the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Roman-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Roman Empire</a>.</p><!--[P25]--><p><span id="ref796053"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Roman-law" class="md-crosslink">Roman law</a>, which stressed the sanctity of contracts, became the basis of treaties. Late in the Republican era, the laws applied by the Romans to foreigners and to foreign envoys were merged with the Greek concept of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/natural-law" class="md-crosslink">natural law</a>, an ideal code applying to all people, to create a “law of nations.” The sanctity of treaties and the law of nations were absorbed by the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Roman-Catholicism" class="md-crosslink">Roman Catholic Church</a> and preserved in the centuries after the Western Roman Empire collapsed, and a foundation was thus provided for the more-sophisticated doctrines of <span id="ref83025"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-law" class="md-crosslink">international law</a> that began to emerge along with the European nation-state a millennium later.</p><!--[P26]--></section></section><section id="ref233759" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The <span id="ref83026"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Middle-Ages" class="md-crosslink">Middle Ages</a></h2><p>When the Western Empire disintegrated in the 5th century <span class="text-smallcaps">ce</span>, most of its diplomatic traditions disappeared. However, even as monarchs negotiated directly with nearby rulers or at a distance through envoys from the 5th through the 9th century, the papacy continued to use <em>legati</em>. Both forms of diplomacy intensified in the next three centuries. Moreover, the eastern half of the Roman Empire continued for nearly 1,000 years as the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Byzantine-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Byzantine Empire</a>. Its court at Constantinople, to which the papacy sent envoys from the mid-5th century, had a department of foreign affairs and a bureau to deal with foreign envoys. Aiming to awe and intimidate foreign envoys, Byzantium’s rulers marked the arrival of diplomats with spectacular ceremonies calculated to suggest greater power than the empire actually possessed.</p><!--[P27]--><span id="ref233759" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233738" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref796054"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Islamic-world" class="md-crosslink">Islam</a></h2><p>Inspired by their religious faith, followers of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Islam" class="md-crosslink">Islam</a> in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Arabia-peninsula-Asia" class="md-crosslink">Arabia</a> conquered significant territory beginning in the 7th century, first by taking Byzantium’s southern and North African provinces and then by uniting Arabs, Persians, and ultimately Turks and other Central Asian peoples in centuries of occasionally bloody conflict with the Christian <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/Byzantines" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="Byzantines">Byzantines</a>. The <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/community" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="community">community</a> of Islam aspired to a single human society in which secular institutions such as the state would have no significant role. In such a society there would be political interaction but no requirement for diplomatic missions between one independent ruler and another. Theoretically, since non-Muslim states eventually would accept the message of Islam, the need for diplomatic exchanges between them and the Islamic community also would be purely temporary. In practice, however, diplomatic missions, both to other Muslim states and to non-Muslim states, existed from the time of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Muhammad" class="md-crosslink">Muhammad</a>, and early Islamic rulers and jurists developed an elaborate set of protections and rules to <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/facilitate" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="facilitate">facilitate</a> the exchange of emissaries. As Muslims came to dominate vast territories in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Africa" class="md-crosslink">Africa</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Asia" class="md-crosslink">Asia</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>, the experience of contention with Byzantium shaped Islamic diplomatic tradition along <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/Byzantine" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> lines.</p><!--[P28]--></section><span id="ref233759" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233739" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref83027"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Byzantine-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Byzantium</a></h2><p>Byzantium produced the first professional diplomats. They were issued written instructions and were enjoined to be polite, to entertain as lavishly as funds permitted, and to sell Byzantine wares to lower their costs and encourage trade. From the 12th century their role as gatherers of information about conditions in their host states became increasingly vital to the survival of the Byzantine state. As its strength waned, timely intelligence from Byzantine diplomats enabled the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/emperor-title" class="md-crosslink">emperors</a> to play foreign nations off against each other. Byzantium’s use of diplomats as licensed spies and its employment of the information they gathered to devise skillful and subtle policies to compensate for a lack of real power inspired neighbouring peoples (e.g., Arabs, Persians, and Turks) as well as others farther away in Rome and the Italian city-states. After the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Byzantine-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Byzantine Empire</a>’s collapse, major elements of its diplomatic tradition lived on in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Ottoman-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Ottoman Empire</a> and in Renaissance Italy.</p><!--[P29]--></section><span id="ref233759" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233740" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">Diplomacy of the <span id="ref83028"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Roman-Catholicism" class="md-crosslink">Roman Catholic</a> Church</h2><p>As Byzantium crumbled, the West revived. Indeed, even in its period of greatest weakness, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Roman-Catholicism" class="md-crosslink">Roman Catholic Church</a> conducted an active diplomacy, especially at Constantinople and in its 13th-century struggle against the Holy Roman emperors. <span id="ref83029"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/papacy" class="md-crosslink">Popes</a> served as arbiters, and papal <span id="ref83030"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/legate-Roman-Catholicism" class="md-crosslink">legates</a> served as peacemakers. The <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/prestige" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="prestige">prestige</a> of the church was such that, at every court, papal emissaries took <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/precedence" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="precedence">precedence</a> over secular envoys, a tradition that continues in countries where Roman Catholicism is the official religion. The Roman emphasis on the sanctity of legates became part of <span id="ref83031"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/canon-law" class="md-crosslink">canon law</a>, and church lawyers developed increasingly elaborate rules governing the status, privileges, and conduct of papal envoys, rules that were adapted later for secular use. Still later, rules devised for late medieval church councils provided guidelines for modern international conferences.</p><!--[P30]--><p>From the 6th century, both legates and (lesser-ranking) <em>nuncii</em> (messengers) carried letters of <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/credence" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="credence">credence</a> to assure the rulers to whom they were accredited of the extent of their authority as agents of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/pope" class="md-crosslink">pope</a>, a practice later adopted for lay envoys. A <span id="ref83032"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/nuncio" class="md-crosslink"><em>nuncius</em></a> (English: nuncio) was a messenger who represented and acted legally for the pope; <em>nuncii</em> could negotiate draft agreements but could not commit the pope without referral. In time, the terms <em>legate</em> and <em>nuncius</em> came to be used for the diplomatic representatives of secular rulers as well as the pope. By the 12th century the secular use of <em>nuncii</em> as diplomatic agents was commonplace.</p><!--[P31]--><p>When diplomacy was confined to nearby states and meetings of rulers were easily arranged, a visiting messenger such as the <em>nuncius</em> <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/sufficed" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="sufficed">sufficed</a>. However, as trade revived, negotiations at a distance became increasingly common. Envoys no longer could refer the details of negotiations to their masters on a timely basis. They therefore needed the discretionary authority to decide matters on their own. To meet this need, in the 12th century the concept of a procurator with <em>plena potens</em> (full powers) was revived from Roman <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/civil-law-Romano-Germanic" class="md-crosslink">civil law</a>. This <span id="ref796055"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/plenipotentiary" class="md-crosslink">plenipotentiary</a> could negotiate and conclude an agreement, but, unlike a <em>nuncius</em>, he could not represent his principal ceremonially. As a result, one emissary was often given both offices.</p><!--[P32]--></section><span id="ref233759" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233741" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref83037"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Venice" class="md-crosslink">Venice</a></h2><p>At the end of the 12th century, the term <em><span id="ref83034"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ambassador" class="md-crosslink">ambassador</a></em> appeared, initially in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Italy" class="md-crosslink">Italy</a>. Derived from the medieval Latin <em>ambactiare</em>, meaning “to go on a mission,” the term was used to describe various envoys, some of whom were not agents of sovereigns. Common in both Italy and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/France" class="md-crosslink">France</a> in the 13th century, it first appeared in English in 1374 in <em><span id="ref796056"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Troilus-and-Criseyde" class="md-crosslink">Troilus and Criseyde</a></em> by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Geoffrey-Chaucer" class="md-crosslink">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>. By the late 15th century, the envoys of secular rulers were commonly called ambassadors, though the papacy continued to send legates and <em>nuncii</em>. Each ambassador carried a letter of credence, though he could not commit his principal unless granted plenipotentiary authority.</p><!--[P33]--><p>The <span id="ref83035"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Crusades" class="md-crosslink">Crusades</a> and the revival of <span id="ref83036"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-trade" class="md-crosslink">trade</a> increased <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>’s contact with the eastern Mediterranean and West Asia. Venice’s location afforded that leading Italian city-state early ties with Constantinople, from which it absorbed major elements of the Byzantine diplomatic system. On the basis of Byzantine precedents, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Venice" class="md-crosslink">Venice</a> gave its envoys written instructions, a practice otherwise unknown in the West, and established a systematic archive. (The Venetian archives contain a registry of all diplomatic documents from 883.) <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Venice" class="md-crosslink">Venice</a> later developed an extensive diplomacy on the Byzantine model, which emphasized the reporting of conditions in the host country. Initially, returning Venetian envoys presented their <em><span id="ref83038"></span>relazione</em> (final report) orally, but, beginning in the 15th century, such reports were presented in writing. Other Italian city-states, followed by France and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Spain" class="md-crosslink">Spain</a>, copied Venetian diplomatic methods and style.</p><!--[P34]--></section></section><section id="ref59478" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The <span id="ref254529"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Renaissance" class="md-crosslink">Renaissance</a> to 1815</h2><span id="ref59478" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233742" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The development of <span id="ref254530"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Italy" class="md-crosslink">Italian</a> diplomacy</h2><p>It is unclear which Italian city-state had the first permanent envoy. In the late <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Middle-Ages" class="md-crosslink">Middle Ages</a> and early <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Renaissance" class="md-crosslink">Renaissance</a> period, most <span id="ref254531"></span>embassies were temporary, lasting from three months to two years. As early as the late 14th and early 15th centuries, however, Venice, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Milan-Italy" class="md-crosslink">Milan</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Mantua-Italy" class="md-crosslink">Mantua</a> sent resident envoys to each other, to the popes, and to the Holy Roman emperors. At this time, envoys generally did not travel with their wives (who were assumed to be indiscreet), but their missions usually employed cooks for purposes of hospitality and to avoid being poisoned. <span id="ref254532"></span>Resident embassies became the norm in Italy in the late 15th century, and after 1500 the practice spread northward. A permanent Milanese envoy to the French court of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Louis-XI" class="md-crosslink">Louis XI</a> arrived in 1463 and was later joined by a Venetian representative. Ambassadors served a variety of roles, including reporting events to their <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/government" class="md-crosslink">government</a> and negotiating with their hosts. In addition, they absorbed the role of commercial consuls, who were not then diplomatic agents.</p><!--[P35]--><p>Italy’s early economic revival, geographic location, and small size fostered the creation of a European state system in microcosm. As the peninsula was fully organized into states, wars were frequent, and the maintenance of an <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/equilibrium" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="equilibrium">equilibrium</a> (“<span id="ref254557"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/balance-of-power" class="md-crosslink">balance of power</a>”) necessitated constant diplomatic interaction. Whereas meetings of rulers aroused expectations and were considered risky, unobtrusive diplomacy by resident envoys was deemed safer and more effective. Thus, the system of permanent agents took root, with members of the upper middle class or younger sons of great families serving as envoys.</p><!--[P36]--><p><span id="ref254533"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Rome" class="md-crosslink">Rome</a> became the centre of Italian diplomacy and of intrigue, information gathering, and spying. Popes received <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ambassador" class="md-crosslink">ambassadors</a> but did not send them. The papal court had the first organized diplomatic corps: the popes addressed the envoys jointly, seated them as a group for ceremonies, and established rules for their collective governance.</p><!--[P37]--><p>As resident missions became the norm, ceremonial and social occasions came to dominate the relations between diplomats and their hosts, especially because the dignity of the sovereign being represented was at stake. Papal envoys took precedence over those of temporal rulers. Beyond that there was little agreement on the relative status of envoys, and there was frequent strife. Pope <span id="ref254534"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Julius-II" class="md-crosslink">Julius II</a> established a list of precedence in 1504, but this did not solve the problem. Spain did not accept inferiority to France; power fluctuated among the states; papal power declined; and the Protestant revolt complicated matters—not least regarding the pope’s own position. By the 16th century the title of ambassador was being used only for envoys of crowned heads and the republic of Venice. Latin remained the international language of diplomacy.</p><!--[P38]--><p>The French <span id="ref254535"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Italian-Wars" class="md-crosslink">invasion of 1494</a> confronted the Italian states with intervention by a power greater than any within their own state system. They were driven to substitute subtle diplomacy and expedient, if short-lived, compromise for the force they lacked. This tendency, plus their enthusiasm for diplomatic <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/nuances" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="nuances">nuances</a> and the 16th-century writings of <span id="ref254536"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Niccolo-Machiavelli" class="md-crosslink">Niccolò Machiavelli</a>, gave Italian diplomacy a reputation for being <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/devious" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="devious">devious</a>. But it was no more so than that of other states, and Machiavelli, himself a Florentine diplomat, argued that an envoy needed <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/integrity" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="integrity">integrity</a>, reliability, and honesty, along with tact and skill in the use of occasional equivocation and selective abridgment of aspects of the truth unfavourable to his cause—views seconded since by virtually every authority.</p><!--[P39]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="88476"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/27/94127-004-31D1C17E.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/27/94127-004-31D1C17E.jpg" alt="Machiavelli, Niccolò" data-width="353" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Machiavelli, Niccolò</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Niccolò Machiavelli, oil painting by Santi di Tito; in the Palazzo Vecchio, Florence.</span><cite>Mondadori Portfolio/age fotostock</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML39]--></section><span id="ref59478" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233743" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The spread of the Italian diplomatic system</h2><p>The 16th-century wars in Italy, the emergence of strong states north of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Alps" class="md-crosslink">Alps</a>, and the Protestant revolt ended the Italian <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Renaissance" class="md-crosslink">Renaissance</a> but spread the Italian system of diplomacy. <span id="ref254537"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Henry-VII-king-of-England" class="md-crosslink">Henry VII</a> of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/England" class="md-crosslink">England</a> was among the first to adopt the Italian diplomatic system, and he initially even used Italian envoys. By the 1520s <span id="ref254538"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Thomas-Cardinal-Wolsey" class="md-crosslink">Thomas Cardinal Wolsey</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Henry-VIII-king-of-England" class="md-crosslink">Henry VIII</a>’s chancellor, had created an English diplomatic service. Under <span id="ref254539"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Francis-I-king-of-France" class="md-crosslink">Francis I</a>, <span id="ref254540"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/France" class="md-crosslink">France</a> adopted the Italian system in the 1520s and had a corps of resident envoys by the 1530s, when the title of “<span id="ref254541"></span>envoy extraordinary” gained currency, originally for special ceremonial missions.</p><!--[P40]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="33806"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/21/8721-004-38EA2B9F.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/21/8721-004-38EA2B9F.jpg" alt="Wolsey, Thomas Cardinal" data-width="352" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Wolsey, Thomas Cardinal</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Thomas Cardinal Wolsey, detail of a painting by Sampson Strong, 1526; in Christ Church, Oxford.</span><cite>Courtesy of the Governing Body of Christ Church, Oxford</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML40]--><p>In the 16th and early 17th centuries, <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/bureaucracies" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="bureaucracies">bureaucracies</a> scarcely existed. Courtiers initially filled this role, but, by the middle of the 16th century, royal secretaries had taken charge of foreign affairs amid their other duties. Envoys remained personal emissaries of one ruler to another. Because they were highly trusted and communications were slow, ambassadors enjoyed considerable freedom of action. Their task was complicated by the ongoing religious wars, which generated distrust, narrowed contacts, and jeopardized the reporting that was essential before newspapers were widespread.</p><!--[P41]--><p>The religious wars of the early 17th century were an Austro-French power struggle. During the <span id="ref254542"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Thirty-Years-War" class="md-crosslink">Thirty Years’ War</a>, <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/innovations" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="innovations">innovations</a> occurred in the theory and practice of international relations. In 1625 the Dutch jurist <span id="ref254543"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Hugo-Grotius" class="md-crosslink">Hugo Grotius</a> published <em>De Jure Belli ac Pacis</em> (<em><span id="ref254544"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/On-the-Law-of-War-and-Peace" class="md-crosslink">On the Law of War and Peace</a></em>), in which the laws of war were most numerous. Grotius deplored the strife of the era, which had undermined the traditional props of customary and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/canon-law" class="md-crosslink">canon law</a>. In an effort to convert the law of nations into a law among nations and to provide it with a new secular rationale acceptable to both sides in the religious quarrel, Grotius fell back on the classical view of natural law and the rule of reason. His book—considered the first definitive work of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-law" class="md-crosslink">international law</a> despite its debt to earlier scholars—enunciated the concepts of state <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sovereignty" class="md-crosslink">sovereignty</a> and the equality of sovereign states, both basic to the modern diplomatic system.</p><!--[P42]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="11254"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/69/11369-004-EE72A58F.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/69/11369-004-EE72A58F.jpg" alt="Hugo Grotius, detail of a portrait by Michiel Janszoon van Mierevelt; in the Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam." data-width="248" data-height="300"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Hugo Grotius, detail of a portrait by Michiel Janszoon van Mierevelt; in the Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam.</span><cite>Courtesy of the Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML42]--></section><span id="ref59478" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233744" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The development of the <span id="ref796027"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/foreign-ministry" class="md-crosslink">foreign ministry</a> and embassies</h2><p>The first modern foreign ministry was established in 1626 in France by <span id="ref254545"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Armand-Jean-du-Plessis-cardinal-et-duc-de-Richelieu" class="md-crosslink">Cardinal Richelieu</a>. Richelieu saw diplomacy as a continuous process of negotiation, arguing that a diplomat should have one master and one policy. He created the Ministry of External Affairs to centralize policy and to ensure his control of envoys as he pursued the <em><span id="ref254546"></span>raison d’état</em> (national interest). Richelieu rejected the view that policy should be based on dynastic or sentimental concerns or a ruler’s wishes, holding instead that the state <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/transcended" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="transcended">transcended</a> crown and land, prince and people, and had interests and needs independent of all these elements. He asserted that the art of government lay in recognizing these interests and acting according to them, regardless of ethical or religious considerations. In this, Richelieu enunciated principles that leaders throughout the world now accept as axioms of statecraft.</p><!--[P43]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="220307"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/75/151775-004-BE2F0EC7.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/75/151775-004-BE2F0EC7.jpg" alt="Cardinal Richelieu" data-width="296" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Cardinal Richelieu</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Full-length portrait of Cardinal Richelieu, French prelate and statesman, 19th century. </span><cite>© iStockphoto/Thinkstock</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML43]--><p>Richelieu’s practice of <em>raison d’état</em> led him to ally Roman Catholic France with the Protestant powers (equally pursuing <em>raison d’état</em>) in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Thirty-Years-War" class="md-crosslink">Thirty Years’ War</a> against France’s great rival, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Austria" class="md-crosslink">Austria</a>. He largely succeeded, for the <span id="ref254547"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Peace-of-Westphalia" class="md-crosslink">Peace of Westphalia</a> of 1648 weakened <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Austria" class="md-crosslink">Austria</a> and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/enhanced" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="enhanced">enhanced</a> French power. The four years of meetings before its signature were the first great international congresses of modern history. Princes attended, but diplomats did most of the work in secret meetings, especially because by this time there was a corps of experienced diplomats who were mutually well acquainted. However, the task of the diplomats was complicated by the need for two simultaneous congresses, because the problem of precedence was otherwise insoluble.</p><!--[P44]--><p>The Treaty of Westphalia did not solve precedence disputes, which reflected rivalry between states. The <span id="ref254548"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Franco-Spanish-War" class="md-crosslink">war between France and Spain</a>, which continued from 1648 to 1659, was partly about this issue. Shortly thereafter, in 1661, there was a diplomatic dispute in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/London" class="md-crosslink">London</a> concerning whether the French ambassador’s <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/carriage-vehicle" class="md-crosslink">carriage</a> would precede that of his Spanish rival. War was narrowly averted, but questions of precedence continued to bedevil European diplomacy. As larger states emerged after the Thirty Years’ War, a network of embassies and legations crisscrossed <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>.</p><!--[P45]--><p>To communicate securely with its own installations, England established the first modern courier service in 1641, and several states used <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/cipher" class="md-crosslink">ciphers</a>. A wide variety of people had been employed as ambassadors, ministers, or residents (a more economical envoy usually reserved for lesser tasks). The glittering court of <span id="ref254549"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Louis-XIV-king-of-France" class="md-crosslink">Louis XIV</a> late in the 17th century transformed this situation dramatically. Because a king’s honour at such a court required that his emissaries be well-born, aristocratic envoys became common, not least because of the expense involved. Also, as kings became better established, nobles were more willing to serve them. Thus, diplomacy became a profession dominated by the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/aristocracy" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="aristocracy">aristocracy</a>. Another result of Louis XIV’s preeminence was that French became the language of diplomacy, superseding Latin. French continued as the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/lingua-franca" class="md-crosslink">lingua franca</a> of diplomacy until the 20th century.</p><!--[P46]--><p>Louis XIV personally directed French foreign policy and read the dispatches of his ambassadors himself. The foreign minister belonged to the Council of State and directed a small ministry and a sizable diplomatic corps under the king’s supervision. Envoys were assigned for three or four years and given letters of credence, instructions, and ciphers for secret correspondence. Because ambassadors chose and paid for their own staff, they were required to have great wealth. Louis XIV’s frequent wars concluded in peace congresses, which were attended by diplomats. To counter the cost of the king’s wars, the French foreign minister stressed commerce and commercial diplomacy.</p><!--[P47]--><p>Some states regularized the position of <span id="ref795995"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/consul-government-official" class="md-crosslink">consuls</a> as state officials, though they were not considered diplomats. The French system was imitated in the 18th century as other major states established foreign ministries. The ambassadors they sent forth were true plenipotentiaries, able to conclude treaties on their own authority. The title of ambassador was used only for the envoys of kings (and for those from <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Venice" class="md-crosslink">Venice</a>). The diplomacy of the time recognized the existence of great powers by according special rank and responsibility to the representatives of these countries. New among these was <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Russia" class="md-crosslink">Russia</a>, which entered European diplomacy in the 18th century. Its diplomatic tradition married elements derived directly from Byzantium to the now essentially mature diplomatic system that had arisen in western <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>.</p><!--[P48]--><p>At the century’s end an independent power of the second rank appeared outside Europe: the <span id="ref83114"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a>. The founders of American diplomacy—people such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Benjamin-Franklin" class="md-crosslink">Benjamin Franklin</a> and <span id="ref254553"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Thomas-Jefferson" class="md-crosslink">Thomas Jefferson</a>—accepted the norms of European diplomacy but declined to wear court dress or to adopt usages they considered unrepublican. To this day, U.S. ambassadors, unlike those of other countries, are addressed not as “Your Excellency” but simply as “Mr. Ambassador.”</p><!--[P49]--><p>By the 18th century diplomacy had begun to generate a sizable <a href="https://www.britannica.com/art/literature" class="md-crosslink">literature</a>, written mostly by its practitioners. Most of these authors argued that to be effective, ambassadors needed to exercise intelligence, trustworthiness, humaneness, foresight, courage, a sense of humour, and sternness (if only to compensate for the not-infrequent lack of these qualities in the national leaders in whose names they acted). One of the earliest such writers, the Dutch diplomat <span id="ref254554"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Abraham-de-Wicquefort" class="md-crosslink">Abraham de Wicquefort</a>, in 1679 termed an envoy “an honourable spy” and “a messenger of peace” who should be charming, silent, and indirect, though, he asserted, deceit was invariably counterproductive. The French diplomat <span id="ref254555"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Francois-de-Callieres" class="md-crosslink">François de Callières</a>, who wrote a manual of diplomacy in 1716 that is still read and regarded by diplomats as a classic, argued in favour of the professionalization of diplomacy, declaring that “even in those cases where success has attended the efforts of an amateur diplomatist, the example must be regarded as an exception, for it is a commonplace of human experience that skilled work requires a skilled workman.” By 1737 another French diplomat-theorist, <span id="ref254556"></span>Antoine Pecquet, had declared diplomacy to be a sacred calling requiring discretion, patience, accurate reporting, and absolute honesty, themes that have been repeated through succeeding centuries.</p><!--[P50]--></section></section><section id="ref233745" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">The Concert of Europe to the outbreak of World War I</h2><span id="ref233745" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233746" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">Balance of power and the Concert of Europe</h2><p>Through the many wars and peace congresses of the 18th century, European diplomacy strove to maintain a balance between five great powers: Britain, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/France" class="md-crosslink">France</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Austria" class="md-crosslink">Austria</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Russia" class="md-crosslink">Russia</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Prussia" class="md-crosslink">Prussia</a>. At the century’s end, however, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/French-Revolution" class="md-crosslink">French Revolution</a>, France’s efforts to export it, and the attempts of <span id="ref254558"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Napoleon-I" class="md-crosslink">Napoleon I</a> to conquer Europe first unbalanced and then overthrew the continent’s state system. After Napoleon’s defeat, the <span id="ref83071"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Congress-of-Vienna" class="md-crosslink">Congress of Vienna</a> was <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/convened" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="convened">convened</a> in 1814–15 to set new boundaries, re-create the balance of power, and guard against future French <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/hegemony" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="hegemony">hegemony</a>. It also dealt with international problems internationally, taking up issues such as rivers, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/slave-trade" class="md-crosslink autoxref">slave trade</a>, and the rules of diplomacy. The <span id="ref83072"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Final-Act-of-Vienna" class="md-crosslink">Final Act of Vienna</a> of 1815, as <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/amended" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="amended">amended</a> at the <span id="ref83075"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Congress-of-Aix-la-Chapelle" class="md-crosslink">Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle</a> (Aachen) in 1818, established four classes of heads of diplomatic missions—precedence within each class being determined by the date of presentation of credentials—and a system for signing treaties in French alphabetical order by country name. Thus ended the battles over precedence. Unwritten rules also were established. At <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Vienna" class="md-crosslink">Vienna</a>, for example, a distinction was made between great powers and “powers with limited interests.” Only great powers exchanged ambassadors. Until 1893 the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> had no ambassadors; like those of other lesser states, its envoys were only ministers.</p><!--[P51]--><p>More unwritten rules were soon developed. Napoleon’s return and second defeat required a new peace treaty with France at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Paris" class="md-crosslink">Paris</a> in November 1815. On that occasion the four great victors (Britain, Austria, Russia, and Prussia) formally signed the <span id="ref83073"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Quadruple-Alliance-Europe-1813-1815" class="md-crosslink">Quadruple Alliance</a>, which called for periodic meetings of the signatories to consult on common interests, to ensure the “repose and prosperity of the Nations,” and to maintain the peace of Europe. This clause, which created a <span id="ref83074"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Concert-of-Europe" class="md-crosslink">Concert of Europe</a>, entailed cooperation and restraint as well as a tacit code: the great powers would make all important decisions; internal changes in any member of the Concert had to be sanctioned by the great powers; the great powers were not to challenge each other; and the Concert would decide all disputes. The Concert thus <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/constituted" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="constituted">constituted</a> a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/rudimentary" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="rudimentary">rudimentary</a> system of international governance by a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/consortium" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="consortium">consortium</a> of great powers.</p><!--[P52]--><p>Initially, meetings of the Concert were attended by rulers, chancellors, and foreign ministers. The first meeting, which was held at Aix-la-Chapelle in 1818, resulted in the admittance of France to the Concert and the secret renewal of the Quadruple Alliance against it. The meeting also refined diplomatic rules and tackled other international questions. Aix was the first international congress held in peacetime and the first to attract coverage by the press, relations with whom were conducted by the secretary-general of the congress. Thus was born the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/public-relations-communications" class="md-crosslink autoxref">public relations</a> aspect of diplomacy and the press communiqué.</p><!--[P53]--><p>Thereafter, congresses met in response to crises. Owing to disputes between the powers, after 1822 the meetings ceased, though the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Concert-of-Europe" class="md-crosslink">Concert of Europe</a> itself continued unobtrusively. Beginning in 1816 an ambassadorial conference was established in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Paris" class="md-crosslink">Paris</a> to address issues arising from the 1815 treaty with France.
