Other conferences of ambassadors followed—usually in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/London" class="md-crosslink">London</a>, Vienna, or Paris—to address specific international problems and to sanction change when it seemed advisable or unavoidable. Diplomats continued to adjust and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/amend" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="amend">amend</a> the European system with conferences, ranging from the meeting held in <span id="ref83076"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Conference-of-London" class="md-crosslink">London</a> in 1830 that <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/endorsed" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="endorsed">endorsed</a> Belgian independence to the meeting in 1912–13, also held in London, to resolve the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Balkan-Wars" class="md-crosslink">Balkan Wars</a>. The Concert was stretched and then disregarded altogether between 1854 and 1870, during the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Crimean-War" class="md-crosslink">Crimean War</a> and the unifications of Italy and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Germany" class="md-crosslink">Germany</a>. The century during which it existed (1815–1914) was generally peaceful, marred only by short, limited wars; the bloodshed of one of these wars, the second war of Italian independence, inspired the creation in the 1860s of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Committee-of-the-Red-Cross" class="md-crosslink autoxref">International Committee for the Relief of the Wounded</a> (later the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Red-Cross-and-Red-Crescent" class="md-crosslink">International Red Cross</a>) as an international nongovernmental agency.</p><!--[P54]--></section><span id="ref233745" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233747" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref254560"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/conference-diplomacy" class="md-crosslink">Conference diplomacy</a> and the impact of democratization</h2><p>After three decades Europe reverted to conference diplomacy at the foreign ministerial level. The <span id="ref83077"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Treaty-of-Paris-1856" class="md-crosslink">Congress of Paris</a> of 1856 not only ended the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Crimean-War" class="md-crosslink">Crimean War</a> but also resulted in the codification of a significant amount of international law. As European powers extended their sway throughout the world, colonies and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sphere-of-influence" class="md-crosslink">spheres of influence</a> in areas remote from Europe came increasingly to preoccupy their diplomacy. <span id="ref83078"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Congress-of-Berlin" class="md-crosslink">Conferences in Berlin</a> in 1878 and 1884–85 prevented <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/conflagrations" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="conflagrations">conflagrations</a> over the so-called “Eastern” and “African” questions—euphemisms, respectively, for intervention on behalf of Christian interests in the decaying <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Ottoman-Empire" class="md-crosslink">Ottoman Empire</a> and the carving up of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Africa" class="md-crosslink">Africa</a> into European-ruled colonies. Furthermore, multilateral diplomacy was institutionalized in a permanent form. The Paris Congress created an International Commission of the Danube to match Vienna’s 1815 Commission of the Rhine and established the Universal Telegraph Union (later the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Telecommunication-Union" class="md-crosslink">International Telecommunication Union</a>). In 1874 the General Postal Union (later the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Universal-Postal-Union" class="md-crosslink">Universal Postal Union</a>) was established. Afterward, specialized agencies like these proliferated. The <span id="ref254559"></span>peace conferences at <span id="ref83079"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/The-Hague" class="md-crosslink">The Hague</a> (1899–1907), which resulted in conventions aimed at codifying the laws of war and encouraging <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/disarmament" class="md-crosslink">disarmament</a>, were <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/harbingers" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="harbingers">harbingers</a> of the future.</p><!--[P55]--><p>During the 19th century the world underwent a series of political transformations, and diplomacy changed with it. In <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a> power shifted from royal courts to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/cabinet-government" class="md-crosslink">cabinets</a>. Kings were replaced by ministers at international meetings, and foreign policy became a matter of increasingly democratized politics. This, plus mass literacy and the advent of inexpensive <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/newspaper" class="md-crosslink">newspapers</a>, made foreign policy and diplomacy concerns of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/public-opinion" class="md-crosslink autoxref">public opinion</a>. Domestic politics thus gained increasing influence over European foreign policy making.</p><!--[P56]--><p>Meanwhile, European <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/culture" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="culture">culture</a> and its diplomatic norms spread throughout the world. Most <span id="ref83080"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Latin-America" class="md-crosslink">Latin American</a> colonies became independent, which increased the number of sovereign states. With their European heritage, Latin American countries adopted the existing system without question, as the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> had done earlier. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/British-Empire" class="md-crosslink">British Empire</a>, through the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/East-India-Company" class="md-crosslink">East India Company</a>, gnawed away at the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Mughal-dynasty" class="md-crosslink">Mughal dynasty</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a>’s many independent states and principalities and then united all of the subcontinent for the first time under a single <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sovereignty" class="md-crosslink">sovereignty</a>. In the middle of the 19th century, an American naval flotilla forced <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Japan" class="md-crosslink">Japan</a> to open its society to the rest of the world. Afterward, Japan embarked on a rapid program of modernization based on the wholesale adoption of Western norms of political and economic behaviour, including European notions of <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/sovereignty" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="sovereignty">sovereignty</a> and diplomatic practice.</p><!--[P57]--></section><span id="ref233745" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233748" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The spread of European diplomatic norms</h2><p>In the late 18th and early 19th centuries, European emissaries to <span id="ref796068"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">China</a> faced demands to prostrate themselves (“<span id="ref796062"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/kowtow" class="md-crosslink">kowtow</a>”) to the Chinese emperor in order to be formally received by him in Beijing, a humiliating practice that Europeans had not encountered since the era of Byzantium. As plenipotentiary representatives of foreign sovereigns, they viewed it as completely inconsistent with the Westphalian concept of sovereign equality. The Chinese, for their part, neither understanding nor accepting diplomatic concepts and practices elaborated in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>, were vexed and insulted by the incivility of Western representatives unwilling to respect the long-established ceremonial requirements of the Chinese court. In the ensuing argument between Western and Chinese concepts of diplomatic <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/protocol" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="protocol">protocol</a>, Europeans prevailed by force of arms. In 1860 British and French forces sacked and pillaged the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/emperor-title" class="md-crosslink">emperor</a>’s summer palace and some areas around <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Beijing" class="md-crosslink">Beijing</a>. They refused to withdraw until the Chinese court had agreed to receive ambassadors on terms consistent with Western practices and to make other <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/concessions" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="concessions">concessions</a>. This was merely one of several Western military interventions undertaken to force Chinese acceptance of Western-dictated terms of engagement with the outside world.</p><!--[P58]--><p>Western diplomacy beyond Europe initially was conducted at a leisurely pace, given the vastly greater distances and times required for <span id="ref83081"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/communication" class="md-crosslink">communication</a>. For non-European countries such as the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a>, this was an unavoidable reality, but, for the European great powers, it was a novelty. Fortunately, the dispatch of far-flung legations developed almost simultaneously with advances in transportation and communications, which made frequent contact possible. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/national-railway" class="md-crosslink">railway</a>, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/telegraph" class="md-crosslink">telegraph</a>, the steamship, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/undersea-cable" class="md-crosslink">undersea cable</a> sped the transmittal of instructions and information. Although the days of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Robert-Stewart-Viscount-Castlereagh-2nd-marquess-of-Londonderry" class="md-crosslink">Robert Stewart, Viscount Castlereagh</a>—who as British foreign secretary negotiated for months at the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Congress-of-Vienna" class="md-crosslink">Congress of Vienna</a> almost without communication with the cabinet in London—were over, the ambassador’s role was more changed than reduced. Lacking instructions and fearing mistakes, earlier emissaries had often done nothing. Similarly, capitals often felt poorly informed about developments of interest to them. (At the dawn of the 19th century, U.S. President Thomas Jefferson famously instructed his secretary of state: “We have heard nothing from our ambassador in Spain for two years. If we do not hear from him this year, let us write him a letter.”) Improvements in technology now made referral to the capital possible and ensured that capitals heard from their envoys abroad, even in the most distant places, on a more frequent and timely basis. Cabinets consulted the ambassador as their “man on the spot” who knew and understood the local conditions, politics, and leaders.</p><!--[P59]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="35285"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/14/42714-004-C6DFA739.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/14/42714-004-C6DFA739.jpg" alt="Stewart, Robert, Viscount Castlereagh" data-width="317" data-height="450"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Stewart, Robert, Viscount Castlereagh</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Robert Stewart, Viscount Castlereagh, detail of a painting by Sir Thomas Lawrence; in the National Portrait Gallery, London.</span><cite>Courtesy of the National Portrait Gallery, London</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML59]--><p>Speedier communication, more involvement in commercial diplomacy as trade became crucial to prosperity, and, especially, the advent of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/typewriter" class="md-crosslink">typewriters</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/mimeograph" class="md-crosslink">mimeograph</a> machines all contributed to a significant increase in the number of diplomatic reports. Yet diplomacy remained a relatively gilded and leisurely profession, one that the British author <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Harold-Nicolson" class="md-crosslink">Harold Nicolson</a> was able to take up shortly before <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-I" class="md-crosslink">World War I</a> in order to give himself time to write books. It also remained a relatively small profession despite the advent of newcomers. Before 1914 there were 14 missions in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Washington-DC" class="md-crosslink">Washington, D.C.</a>; by the beginning of the 21st century, the number of missions had increased more than 12-fold. Diplomats were gentlemen who knew each other and shared a similar <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/education" class="md-crosslink">education</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/ideology-society" class="md-crosslink">ideology</a>, and culture. They saw themselves as an elite and carefully upheld the fiction that they still were personal envoys of one monarch to another.</p><!--[P60]--></section></section><section id="ref233749" data-level="2"><h2 class="h2">Diplomacy since World War I</h2><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233750" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The <span id="ref83084"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Soviet-Union" class="md-crosslink">Soviet</a> model</h2><p><span id="ref83082"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-I" class="md-crosslink">World War I</a> accelerated many changes in diplomacy. Sparked by the world war, the <span id="ref83083"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Russian-Revolution-of-1917" class="md-crosslink">Russian Revolution of 1917</a> produced a great power regime that rejected the views of the Western world and that used political language—including the terms <em>democracy</em>, <em><span id="ref83086"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/propaganda" class="md-crosslink">propaganda</a></em>, and <em>subversion</em>—in new ways. The communist government of the new <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Soviet-Union" class="md-crosslink">Soviet Union</a> abolished diplomatic ranks and published the secret treaties it found in the czarist archives. In so doing it sought not only to contrive a dramatic contrast to the aristocratic traditions of European diplomacy but also to discredit the cozy dealings between rulers that had so often taken place without regard to the interests or views of those they ruled or affected. Without delay the People’s Commissariat of Foreign Affairs (known by its Russian <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/acronym" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="acronym">acronym</a>, the Narkomindel) organized a press bureau and a bureau for international revolutionary <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/propaganda" class="md-crosslink">propaganda</a>. As Russia entered peace negotiations with <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Germany" class="md-crosslink">Germany</a>, it substituted <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/propaganda" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="propaganda">propaganda</a> for the power it lacked, appealing openly to the urban workers of other states to exert pressure on their <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/government" class="md-crosslink">governments</a>. It also established the Communist International (also called the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Third-International" class="md-crosslink">Third International</a>) as a nominally independent entity that meddled in the politics of capitalist countries in ways no embassy could.</p><!--[P61]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233751" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The League of Nations and the revival of conference diplomacy</h2><p>Despite its risks and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/inherent" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="inherent">inherent</a> complexity, conference diplomacy was revived during World War I and continued afterward, especially during the 1920s. Following the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/armistice-law" class="md-crosslink">armistice</a> that ended the war, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Paris-Peace-Conference" class="md-crosslink">Paris Peace Conference</a> took place amid much publicity, which was intensified by the newsreels made of the event. United States President <span id="ref83088"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Woodrow-Wilson" class="md-crosslink">Woodrow Wilson</a> had enunciated his <span id="ref83089"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Fourteen-Points" class="md-crosslink">peace program</a> in January 1918, including “open <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/covenants" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="covenants">covenants</a> of peace openly arrived at” as a major goal for diplomacy in the post-World War I period. His phrasemaking, which entangled process and result, caused confusion. Hundreds of journalists went to the <span id="ref83090"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Paris-Peace-Conference" class="md-crosslink">conference</a> only to discover that all but the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/plenary" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="plenary">plenary</a> sessions were closed. Wilson had intended that the results of diplomatic negotiations be made public, with treaties published and approved by legislatures. He largely achieved this goal, as the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/Covenant" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="Covenant">Covenant</a> of the <span id="ref83091"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/League-of-Nations" class="md-crosslink">League of Nations</a>—one of the key treaties set out for signature at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Versailles-France" class="md-crosslink">Versailles</a> at the end of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Paris" class="md-crosslink">Paris</a> conference—required that treaties be registered at the League before they became binding.</p><!--[P62]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="231275"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/48/71448-004-9BCD8C64.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/48/71448-004-9BCD8C64.jpg" alt="Lloyd George, David; Orlando, Vittorio; Clemenceau, Georges; Wilson, Woodrow: Paris Peace Conference" data-width="407" data-height="350"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Lloyd George, David; Orlando, Vittorio; Clemenceau, Georges; Wilson, Woodrow: Paris Peace Conference</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Britain's David Lloyd George, Italy's Vittorio Orlando, France's Georges Clemenceau, and Woodrow Wilson from the United States at Versailles.</span><cite>National Archives, Washington, D.C.</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML62]--><p>The Paris conference adopted many of the Congress of Vienna’s procedures, including the differentiation of “powers with general interests” and “powers with special interests,” private meetings of heads of great-power delegations, and the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/convening" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="convening">convening</a> of a Conference of Ambassadors afterward in Paris. The peace conference, the treaties, and the later conferences were conducted bilingually in English and French after the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> joined Britain in world councils. As at <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Vienna" class="md-crosslink">Vienna</a>, political leaders attended, but kings and princes were strikingly absent in an era of cabinet government and widening electorates. Even more than at Vienna, nongovernmental organizations, most representing national entities seeking independence, sought a hearing at the court of the great powers. Ultimately, some European peoples gained independence, which resulted in an increase in the number of sovereign states.</p><!--[P63]--><p>The chief <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/innovation" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="innovation">innovation</a> of the peace negotiations was the creation of the League of Nations as the first permanent major <span id="ref83092"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-organization" class="md-crosslink">international organization</a>, with a secretariat of international civil servants. The League introduced <span id="ref83093"></span>parliamentary diplomacy in a two-chamber body, acknowledging the equality of states in its lower house and the supremacy of great powers in its upper one. As neither chamber had much power, however, the sovereignty of members was not infringed. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/League-of-Nations" class="md-crosslink">League of Nations</a> sponsored conferences—especially on economic questions and disarmament—and supervised specialized agencies (e.g., the Universal Postal Union). New specialized agencies were established to handle new areas of diplomacy. The <span id="ref83094"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Labour-Organization" class="md-crosslink">International Labour Organization</a> addressed domestic issues and included nongovernmental representatives, and the <span id="ref83095"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Permanent-Mandates-Commission" class="md-crosslink">Mandates Commission</a> exercised slight supervision over colonies of the defeated powers, which had been distributed to the victors technically as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/mandate-League-of-Nations" class="md-crosslink">mandates</a> of the League.</p><!--[P64]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="92282"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/52/93152-004-BF089863.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/52/93152-004-BF089863.jpg" alt="League of Nations" data-width="550" data-height="402"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">League of Nations</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Delegates attending a League of Nations meeting, <em>c.</em> 1930.</span><cite>Central Press/Hulton Archives/Getty Images</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML64]--><p>Despite the presence of a Latin American bloc and a few independent or quasi-independent states of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Africa" class="md-crosslink">Africa</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Asia" class="md-crosslink">Asia</a>, the League of Nations was a European club. Diplomats became orators again in the halls of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Geneva-Switzerland" class="md-crosslink">Geneva</a>, but the topics of parliamentary diplomacy were often trivial. Decisions taken in public were rehearsed in secret sessions. On important matters foreign ministers attending League councils met privately in hotel rooms. In <span id="ref954317"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Corfu-incident" class="md-crosslink">1923</a> the League revealed its impotence by dodging action in the <span id="ref83096"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Corfu" class="md-crosslink">Corfu</a> crisis, in which Italian troops occupied the Greek island following the murder of an Italian general on Greek soil. In later years the League failed to improve its record in dealing with international crises.</p><!--[P65]--><p>The weakness of the League of Nations was aggravated by the absence of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a>, whose Senate refused to ratify the peace treaties by which the League was created. The Senate’s inaction raised questions about the country’s reliability—the basis of effective diplomacy—and drew attention to the blurring of the line between foreign and domestic policy and, in the view of some, the irresponsibility of democratic electorates. The public conceived of diplomacy as a kind of athletic contest, cheering its side and booing the opponents, seeking great victories and humiliation of the foe, and fixing on the next score and not on the long term. This attitude rendered good diplomacy—which is based on compromise, mutual advantage, and lasting interests—extremely difficult.</p><!--[P66]--><p>In <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>, where electorates were constantly preoccupied with foreign policy, this problem was most <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/acute" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="acute">acute</a>. Statesmen, trailed by the popular press, engaged in personal diplomacy at frequent conferences. Foreign offices, diplomats, and quiet negotiation were eclipsed as prime ministers and their staffs executed policy in a blaze of publicity. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/government" class="md-crosslink">Governments</a>, led by Britain and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Germany" class="md-crosslink">Germany</a>, manipulated this publicity to influence public opinion in favour of their policies. As the masses became concerned with such matters, unprecedented steps were taken to bribe the foreign press, to plant stories, and to use public occasions for propaganda speeches aimed at foreign audiences. Because public opinion set the <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/parameters" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="parameters">parameters</a> in which foreign policy operated in democratic societies, these efforts had an effect.</p><!--[P67]--><p>Despite these changes, the “new diplomacy” of the early 20th century was, in fact, not so new. For all the oratory at Geneva, the summits of the 1920s, and the specialized conferences and agencies, the negotiating process remained the same. Talks continued to be held in secret, and usually only their results were announced to the public. Meanwhile, diplomats deplored the decline of elite influence and the effects of expanded democracy—e.g., press scrutiny, public attention, and the involvement of politicians—on the diplomatic process.</p><!--[P68]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233752" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref83097"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/totalitarianism" class="md-crosslink">Totalitarian</a> regimes</h2><p>Diplomacy was equally affected by the advent of totalitarian regimes with strong ideologies; more often than not, these regimes honoured established diplomatic rules only when it suited them, and they generally <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/eschewed" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="eschewed">eschewed</a> negotiation and compromise. The government of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Soviet-Union" class="md-crosslink">Soviet Union</a>, for example, viewed all capitalist states as enemies. Especially under the leadership of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Joseph-Stalin" class="md-crosslink">Joseph Stalin</a>, it used each <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/concession" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="concession">concession</a> it won as a basis to press for another, and it viewed diplomacy as war, not as a process of mutual compromise. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Nazi-Party" class="md-crosslink">Nazi</a> Germany under <span id="ref83098"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Adolf-Hitler" class="md-crosslink">Adolf Hitler</a> was equally indifferent to accommodation and Western opinion once it achieved rearmament; Hitler signed treaties with the intention of keeping them only as long as the terms suited him, regarded with <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/contempt" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="contempt">contempt</a> those who tried to accommodate him, and cowed foreign leaders with tantrums and threats.</p><!--[P69]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233753" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref796064"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/summit-diplomacy" class="md-crosslink">Summit diplomacy</a></h2><p>After a lull the tensions of the 1930s revived conference diplomacy, which continued during <span id="ref83099"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a>. Thereafter, summit meetings between heads of government became the norm as technology again quickened the tempo of diplomacy. In the 1930s statesmen began to telephone each other, a practice that was epitomized in the 1960s by the Soviet-American “hot line.” Similarly, the flights of British Prime Minister <span id="ref83100"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Neville-Chamberlain" class="md-crosslink">Neville Chamberlain</a> to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Germany" class="md-crosslink">Germany</a> in 1938, which resulted in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Munich-Agreement" class="md-crosslink">Munich agreement</a> that allowed Germany to annex the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Sudetenland" class="md-crosslink">Sudetenland</a> in western Czechoslovakia, started a trend in diplomacy. With <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/airplane" class="md-crosslink">airplanes</a> at their disposal, leaders met often in the postwar world. As Kojo Debrah, a Ghanaian diplomat, later remarked, “Radio enables people to hear all evil, television enables them to see all evil, and the jet plane enables them to go off and do all evil.”</p><!--[P70]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="144711"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/57/131357-004-B56C5BB0.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/57/131357-004-B56C5BB0.jpg" alt="Mussolini, Benito; Hitler, Adolf; Chamberlain, Neville" data-width="550" data-height="399"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Mussolini, Benito; Hitler, Adolf; Chamberlain, Neville</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">(Left to right) Italian leader Benito Mussolini, German Chancellor Adolf Hitler, a German interpreter, and British Prime Minister Neville Chamberlain meeting in Munich, Germany, September 29, 1938.</span><cite>Tramonto—age fotostock/Imagestate</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML70]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233754" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3"><span id="ref83104"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/decolonization" class="md-crosslink">Decolonization</a> and the beginnings of the <span id="ref83101"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Cold-War" class="md-crosslink">Cold War</a></h2><p>After <span id="ref83103"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a> the world divided into two tight blocs, one dominated by the United States and one by the Soviet Union, with a fragile <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/nonaligned-movement" class="md-crosslink autoxref">nonaligned movement</a> (mostly of newly independent countries) lying precariously in between. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Cold-War" class="md-crosslink">Cold War</a> took place under the threat of nuclear <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/catastrophe" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="catastrophe">catastrophe</a> and gave rise to two major alliances—the <span id="ref83108"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/North-Atlantic-Treaty-Organization" class="md-crosslink">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a>, led by the United States, and the <span id="ref796067"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Warsaw-Pact" class="md-crosslink">Warsaw Pact</a>, led by the Soviet Union—along with a conventional and nuclear <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/arms-race" class="md-crosslink autoxref">arms race</a>, endless disarmament negotiations, much conference diplomacy, many summits, and periodic <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/crisis-management-government" class="md-crosslink autoxref">crisis management</a>, a form of negotiation aimed at living with a problem, not solving it. As a result, a premium was placed on the diplomatic art of continuing to talk until a crisis ceased to boil.</p><!--[P71]--><p><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-I" class="md-crosslink">World War I</a> had produced a few new <span id="ref83102"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/state-sovereign-political-entity" class="md-crosslink">states</a> as eastern European empires crumbled. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a> sounded the death knell for global empires. The immediate postwar period saw the reemergence into full independence of several great civilizations that the age of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/imperialism" class="md-crosslink">imperialism</a> had placed under generations of European tutelage. These reborn countries had taken to heart the doctrines of European diplomacy. With the zeal of new converts, they were, in many ways, more insistent on the concepts of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/sovereignty" class="md-crosslink">sovereignty</a>, territorial integrity, and noninterference in internal affairs than their former colonial masters now were.</p><!--[P72]--><p>After a long struggle for independence, Indians formed two proudly <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/assertive" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="assertive">assertive</a> but mutually antagonistic states, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Pakistan" class="md-crosslink">Pakistan</a>. China’s century-long humiliation at the hands of the West exploded in a series of violent revolutions seeking to restore the country to wealth, power, and a place of dignity internationally. In 1949 <span id="ref796099"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Mao-Zedong" class="md-crosslink">Mao Zedong</a> proclaimed that, with the founding of his <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">People’s Republic of China</a>, the Chinese people had once again “stood up”; but, with U.S. support, Mao’s defeated rival in the Chinese civil war, <span id="ref796069"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Chiang-Kai-shek" class="md-crosslink">Chiang Kai-shek</a>, continued for two decades to speak for China in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/United-Nations" class="md-crosslink">United Nations</a> (UN). The question of China’s international representation became one of the great diplomatic issues of the 1950s and ’60s. The states and principalities of the Arab world resumed their independence and then insisted, over the objections of their former colonial masters, on exercising full sovereignty throughout their own territories, as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Egypt" class="md-crosslink">Egypt</a> did with respect to the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Suez-Canal" class="md-crosslink">Suez Canal</a>. Anti-imperialist <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/sentiment" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="sentiment">sentiment</a> soon made colonialism globally unacceptable. By the late 1950s and ’60s, new states, mainly in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Africa" class="md-crosslink">Africa</a>, were being established on an almost monthly basis.</p><!--[P73]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233755" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The <span id="ref83105"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/United-Nations" class="md-crosslink">United Nations</a> and the changing world order</h2><p>The UN, which replaced the League of Nations in 1946, was founded with 51 members. By the beginning of the 21st century, its membership had nearly quadrupled, though not all the world’s countries had joined. The new states were often undeveloped and technologically weak, with a limited pool of educated elites for the establishment of a modern diplomatic corps. After the larger colonies gained independence, smaller ones, where this problem was more acute, followed suit. The trend continued until even “<span id="ref796070"></span>microstates” of small area and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/population-biology-and-anthropology" class="md-crosslink">population</a> became sovereign. (For example, at its independence in 1968, Nauru had a population of fewer than 7,000.)</p><!--[P74]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="220164"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/23/122123-004-D2F62564.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/23/122123-004-D2F62564.jpg" alt="United Nations headquarters" data-width="550" data-height="382"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">United Nations headquarters</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">United Nations headquarters (building on left), New York, New York.</span><cite>© Mario Savoia/Shutterstock.com</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML74]--><p>These small new states, which achieved independence suddenly, were unable to conduct much diplomacy at first. Many of them accredited ambassadors only to the former colonial power, a key neighbouring state, and the UN. For financial reasons envoys often were sent only to the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/European-Community-European-economic-association" class="md-crosslink">European Community</a> (EC), the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Commonwealth-association-of-states" class="md-crosslink">Commonwealth</a> Secretariat, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/OPEC" class="md-crosslink">Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries</a> (OPEC), or major powers that might extend military and financial assistance. Over time, the larger of the newly independent states built sizable foreign services modeled on that of the former colonial power or those of the similarly organized services of <span id="ref796022"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Brazil" class="md-crosslink">Brazil</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a>, which were not <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/complicit" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="complicit">complicit</a> in colonialism. (The Brazilian foreign ministry and diplomatic service are organized and staffed along European lines; they have long had reputations as the most professional such organizations in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Latin-America" class="md-crosslink">Latin America</a>. The Indian Foreign Service, modeled on the highly respected Indian Administrative Service and initially staffed from its ranks, quickly emerged as a practitioner of competent diplomacy by a nonaligned, non-Western potential great power.) The microstates mounted a few tiny missions and experimented with joint representation and shared facilities, multiple accreditation of one envoy to several capitals, and meeting with foreign envoys in their own capitals. A very few nominally independent states had no foreign ministry and relied on regional powers to represent them.</p><!--[P75]--><p>The new states shared the diplomatic forms of the industrialized <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/democracies" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="democracies">democracies</a> of the West but not their political culture. Many new states were ill at ease with the values of their former colonial masters and cast about for <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/alternatives" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="alternatives">alternatives</a> drawn from their own histories and national experiences. Others accepted Western norms but <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/castigated" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="castigated">castigated</a> the West for hypocrisy and challenged it to live up to its own ideals. Envoys began to appear in Western capitals dressed in <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/indigenous" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="indigenous">indigenous</a> regalia to symbolize their assertion of ancient non-Western cultural identities. As they gained a majority at the UN, the newly independent states fundamentally altered the organization’s stance toward colonies, racial issues, and indigenous peoples. Beyond the East-West division of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Cold-War" class="md-crosslink">Cold War</a>, there developed a “North-South” divide between the wealthier former imperial powers of the north and their less-developed former colonies, many of which called for a worldwide redistribution of wealth.</p><!--[P76]--><p>The UN was no more successful at healing the North-South rift than it was at healing the East-West one. It was, according to former Indian permanent representative Arthur Lall, “a forum and not a force.” Useful mainly for its specialized agencies and as a forum for propaganda and a venue for quiet contacts, it played only a marginal role in major questions and conflicts, though secretaries-general and their deputies made intense efforts to solve serious but secondary problems such as the resettlement of refugees and persons displaced by war. In the end, the UN has remained only, as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Dag-Hammarskjold" class="md-crosslink">Dag Hammarskjöld</a>, UN secretary-general from 1953 to 1961, remarked, “a complement to the normal diplomatic machinery of the governments” that are its members, not a substitute.</p><!--[P77]--><p>Regional organizations sometimes were more successful. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/European-Union" class="md-crosslink">European Union</a> (EU) was effective in promoting <span id="ref83107"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/international-trade" class="md-crosslink">trade</a> and cooperation with member states, and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/African-Union" class="md-crosslink">Organization of African Unity</a> and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Arab-League" class="md-crosslink">Arab League</a> enhanced the international bargaining power of regional groupings of new states by providing a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/coherent" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="coherent">coherent</a> foreign policy and diplomatic <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/strategy-military" class="md-crosslink autoxref">strategy</a>. By contrast, the extreme political, economic, and cultural <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/diversity" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="diversity">diversity</a> of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Asia" class="md-crosslink">Asia</a> made it harder to organize effectively; the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Organization-of-American-States" class="md-crosslink">Organization of American States</a> suffered from the enormous imbalance between the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> and its smaller, poorer, and less-powerful members; and the nonaligned movement was too <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/disparate" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="disparate">disparate</a> for long-term cohesion. None of these entities solved the problem of harmonizing the views of the industrialized democracies, the Soviet bloc, and those newly independent countries struggling for wealth, power, and cultural identity.</p><!--[P78]--><p>The exponential growth in the number of states complicated diplomacy by requiring countries—especially the major powers—to staff many different diplomatic missions at once. As state, transnational, and quasi-diplomatic entities proliferated, so did the functions of diplomacy. Although leaders met often, there was more, not less, for diplomats to do. Thus, the size of the missions of major powers increased enormously, to the point where some U.S. diplomatic missions were three times larger than the foreign ministry of the state to which they were accredited.</p><!--[P79]--><p>Subnational entities, representing peoples aspiring to statehood or to the creation of radically different regimes in their homelands, also complicated the crowded international scene. Foremost among these entities was the <span id="ref83106"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Palestine-Liberation-Organization" class="md-crosslink">Palestine Liberation Organization</a> (PLO), which had observer status at the UN, membership in the Arab League, and envoys in most of the world’s capitals, many with diplomatic status. The <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/African-National-Congress" class="md-crosslink">African National Congress</a> (ANC) and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/SWAPO-Party-of-Namibia" class="md-crosslink">South West African People’s Organization</a> (SWAPO) also conducted a long and varied diplomacy before achieving power in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/South-Africa" class="md-crosslink">South Africa</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Namibia" class="md-crosslink">Namibia</a>, respectively.</p><!--[P80]--><p>New topics of diplomacy also abounded, including economic and military aid, commodity-price stabilization, food sales, aviation, and allocations of radio frequencies. Career diplomats tended to be generalists drawn from foreign ministries, and specialists increasingly came from other agencies as <span id="ref83020"></span>attachés or <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/counselors" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="counselors">counselors</a>. Disarmament negotiations, for example, required specialized knowledge beyond the scope of military attachés. Environmental abuse gave rise to a host of topics, such as the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Law-of-the-Sea" class="md-crosslink">law of the sea</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/global-warming" class="md-crosslink">global warming</a>, and means of preventing or abating pollution. The complexity of diplomatic missions increased accordingly. By the 1960s, for example, U.S. missions had instituted “<span id="ref796071"></span>country teams,” including the ambassador and the heads of all attached missions, which met at least once each week to unify policy and reporting efforts and to prevent different elements under the ambassador from working at cross-purposes.</p><!--[P81]--><p>Not only were there new tasks for diplomacy to perform, but there was also a new emphasis on old tasks. The widening <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Cold-War" class="md-crosslink">Cold War</a> entailed more espionage, of which ambassadors were officially ignorant but which was conducted by attachés and chauffeurs alike; thus, large embassies appeared in small but strategic countries. <span id="ref83111"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/propaganda" class="md-crosslink">Propaganda</a>, the export of officially sanctioned information, and so-called “<span id="ref796072"></span>cultural diplomacy”—as typified by the international tours of Russian dance companies and the cultural programs of the Alliance Française, the British Council, and various American libraries—expanded as well. Cold War competition also extended to international arms transfers. Gifts or sales of weapons and military training were a means of influencing foreign armed forces and consolidating long-term relationships with key elements of foreign governments. The increasing complexity and expense of modern weapons systems also made military exports essential for preserving industrial capacity and employment in the arms industries of the major powers. Diplomats thus became arms merchants, competing with allies and enemies alike for sales to their host governments.</p><!--[P82]--><p>The multiplicity of diplomatic tasks reflected a world that was not only more interdependent but also more fragmented and divided. This dangerous combination led to a search for a new international system to manage the Cold War in order to prevent a nuclear holocaust. Neither the UN nor the Western policy of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/containment-foreign-policy" class="md-crosslink">containment</a> provided an answer. As the two blocs congealed, a balance of terror in the 1960s was followed by an era of détente in the 1970s and then by a return to <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/deterrence" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="deterrence">deterrence</a> in the 1980s. But the 45 years of the Cold War did not produce an organizing principle of any duration. Great power conflict was conducted by <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/proxy" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="proxy">proxy</a> through client states in developing areas. Wars, which were numerous but small, were not declared, and diplomatic relations often continued during the fighting.</p><!--[P83]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233756" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">New styles of diplomacy</h2><p>One result of the breakdown of old <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/premises" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="premises">premises</a>, mainly in new states, was that diplomatic immunity was <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/breached" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="breached">breached</a>, and diplomacy became a hazardous career. Disease was no longer the chief killer of diplomats, nor was overindulgence at court; the new hazards were murder, maiming, and kidnapping. Diplomats were a target because they represented states and symbolized privileged elites. Security precautions at embassies were doubled and redoubled but were never sufficient if host governments turned a blind eye to <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/breaches" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="breaches">breaches</a> of <span id="ref83109"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/extraterritoriality" class="md-crosslink">extraterritoriality</a>. As the 20th century drew to a close, attacks on diplomatic missions and diplomats grew in scale and frequency. <span id="ref83110"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/terrorism" class="md-crosslink">Terrorists</a> succeeded in taking the staffs of some diplomatic missions <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/hostage-warfare" class="md-crosslink autoxref">hostage</a> and in blowing up others, with great loss of life. Some embassies came to resemble fortresses.</p><!--[P84]--><p>Some new states also adopted the Soviet tactic of offensive behaviour as a tool of policy. The newest “new diplomacy” appealed, as the Soviets had done during the interwar period, over the heads of government to people in the opponent’s camp; it tried to discredit governments by attributing ugly motives; and it sometimes trumpeted maximum demands in calculatedly offensive language as conditions for negotiation. Public diplomacy of this ilk was often noisy, <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/bellicose" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="bellicose">bellicose</a>, and self-righteous. The elaborate courtesy of sharply understated, unpublished notes wherein a government “viewed with concern” to convey strong objection was employed by only part of the diplomatic community. The use of <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/derogatory" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="derogatory">derogatory</a> terms such as <em>war criminal</em>, <em>imperialist</em>, <em>neocolonialist</em>, <em>hegemon</em>, <em>racist</em>, and <em>mass murderer</em> not surprisingly proved more likely to enrage than to conciliate those to whom these terms were applied.</p><!--[P85]--><p>As diplomacy raised its voice in public, propaganda, abetted by <span id="ref83112"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/communication" class="md-crosslink">technology</a>, became a key tool. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Radio-Free-Europe" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Radio Free Europe</a> and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Voice-of-America" class="md-crosslink">Voice of America</a> broadcast one message to the communist bloc; <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/proselytizing" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="proselytizing">proselytizing</a> Christian churches and so-called “national liberation movements” capitalized upon transistor radios to spread their messages to other areas. In cities, <span id="ref796074"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/television-technology" class="md-crosslink">television</a> became crucial, as images provided an immediacy that words alone could not convey. Statesmen lost no opportunity to be filmed, and ambassadors emerged from the shadows to appear on news programs or before legislative committees to expound their country’s policy. Mass demonstrations were staged for the benefit of television and featured banners in English, which had become the most important international language. When the United States invaded <span id="ref796077"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Panama" class="md-crosslink">Panama</a> in 1989, the Soviet Union protested on the American-owned television company <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Cable-News-Network" class="md-crosslink">Cable News Network</a>, which was watched by most foreign ministries and world leaders.</p><!--[P86]--><p>The disagreement on how to conduct diplomacy applied also to who should practice it. In the 1970s the United States, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Australia" class="md-crosslink">Australia</a>, and some other industrialized democracies (as well as South Africa) broadened recruitment beyond the old elites and emphasized the development of foreign services representative of their populations’ ethnic diversity. Others, such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Brazil" class="md-crosslink">Brazil</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/France" class="md-crosslink">France</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Japan" class="md-crosslink">Japan</a>, continued to recruit self-consciously elite services. China and the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Soviet-Union" class="md-crosslink">Soviet Union</a> continued to emphasize political <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/criteria" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="criteria">criteria</a> as well as <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/intellectual" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="intellectual">intellectual</a> skills. Overall, however, embassy positions, from the ambassadorial level down, increasingly were filled by professional diplomats. Only the United States and a handful of other countries continued the practice of appointing wealthy amateurs as ambassadors, treating the most senior diplomatic positions as political spoils to be conferred on financial contributors after each election.</p><!--[P87]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233757" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The role of <span id="ref83087"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/women" class="md-crosslink">women</a></h2><p>Famous female political leaders such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Cleopatra-queen-of-Egypt" class="md-crosslink">Cleopatra VII</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Isabella-I-queen-of-Spain" class="md-crosslink">Isabella I</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Elizabeth-I" class="md-crosslink">Elizabeth I</a> were enormously influential in the history of diplomatic relations, but historically women largely played a secondary—but substantial—role as the wives of diplomats. Without large fortunes or many servants, diplomatic wives were forced to shoulder greater burdens as they coped with a nomadic lifestyle, housewifery, hectic social schedules, and endless cooking for obligatory entertaining. The strain became so severe that many ambassadors retired early. In response, Japan adopted the practice of paying diplomatic wives a salary to compensate them for the time they spent entertaining. In 1972 the United States stopped evaluating wives in rating their spouses; entertaining and attending functions were no longer required, though they were still expected. Diplomatic wives also increasingly wished to pursue their own careers. Some of these were portable; if not, efforts were made with host countries to permit employment.</p><!--[P88]--><p>In 1923 the Soviet Union became the first country to name a woman as head of a diplomatic mission. The United States, which began admitting women into the newly established American career service only in 1925, followed a decade later by appointing a woman as minister to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Denmark" class="md-crosslink">Denmark</a>. France permitted a woman to enter its diplomatic service by examination in 1930, though at the time it still did not appoint women as heads of missions.</p><!--[P89]--><p>After <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a>, increasing numbers of women were making a career of diplomacy, and more women became ambassadors, both by political appointment and by career progression. Despite these changes, some countries, particularly in the developing world, continued not to hire women as diplomats, and sending women envoys to them was deemed unwise. In 1970, for example, the Vatican rejected a proposed minister from <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/West-Germany" class="md-crosslink">West Germany</a> because she was female. With these exceptions, however, women became an accepted and rapidly growing minority in the diplomatic, including the ambassadorial, ranks. As the 20th century closed, a number of American women, some accompanied by dependent spouses, were serving as ambassadors in Arab and Islamic countries long considered inhospitable to women.</p><!--[P90]--><p>Before this trend began, women seemed to face almost insuperable difficulties in combining <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/marriage" class="md-crosslink">marriage</a> with the nomadic career of diplomacy. After 1971 the U.S. Foreign Service no longer required women to resign upon marriage, but if the husband’s profession was not easily movable, problems arose. These problems were particularly pronounced for “<span id="ref796078"></span>tandem couples,” in which both husband and wife were in the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/foreign-service" class="md-crosslink">Foreign Service</a>. Since postings together to large embassies or to a department headquarters could not always be arranged, husband and wife often would alternate in taking leave when not posted in <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/adjacent" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="adjacent">adjacent</a> countries. Despite these problems, at the end of the 20th century, the U.S. Foreign Service employed more than 500 tandem couples, including more than one pair serving simultaneously as ambassadors.</p><!--[P91]--></section><span id="ref233749" data-level="2"></span><section id="ref233758" data-level="3"><h2 class="h3">The end of bipolarity</h2><p>In 1989, when the Cold War sputtered to a close, there were more than 7,000 diplomatic missions worldwide, most of which were embassies and thus headed by ambassadors. Between <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-I" class="md-crosslink">World War I</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a>, a few lesser states had been allowed to accredit embassies, but when the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> elevated Latin American missions in the 1940s, a trickle became a flood. Soon legations were the exception, and, by the last quarter of the 20th century, they had disappeared. In addition, numerous often highly specialized international organizations and an array of regional entities, some of them supranational, also now received and sent envoys of ambassadorial rank. For example, some states accredited three ambassadors to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Brussels" class="md-crosslink">Brussels</a>: to the Belgian government, to the EU, and to NATO.</p><!--[P92]--><p>Meanwhile, the already bewildering variety of tasks assigned to overburdened diplomatic missions continued to grow. The emergence of transnational legal issues such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/terrorism" class="md-crosslink">terrorism</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/organized-crime" class="md-crosslink">organized crime</a>, drug trafficking, international smuggling of immigrants and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/refugee" class="md-crosslink">refugees</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/human-rights" class="md-crosslink">human rights</a> increasingly involved embassies in close <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/liaison" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="liaison">liaison</a> with local <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/police" class="md-crosslink">police</a> and prosecutors. As the 20th century came to an end, however, the number of diplomatic missions maintained by independent states began to decline under the influence of various factors, including budgetary constraints, the growing European practice of joint diplomatic representation by two or more members of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/European-Union" class="md-crosslink">EU</a> in the capitals of foreign states of relatively little interest to <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Europe" class="md-crosslink">Europe</a>, greater willingness to accredit ambassadors simultaneously to several regional states, and diminishing domestic interest in foreign affairs.</p><!--[P93]--><p>Even as the number of embassies and diplomats devoted to the conduct of bilateral relations contracted, international organizations and conferences attempting to regulate transnational affairs continued to proliferate. Indeed, the number of nongovernmental entities attempting to influence the work of such organizations and conferences grew even more rapidly: churches, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Committee-of-the-Red-Cross" class="md-crosslink">International Red Cross</a> and similar service and relief organizations, multinational corporations, trade unions, and a host of special interest groups and professional organizations all developed lobbying efforts aimed at advancing specific transnational agendas. By the beginning of the 21st century, there were thousands of nongovernmental organizations accredited as observers to specialized agencies of the UN and other international organizations in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Geneva-Switzerland" class="md-crosslink">Geneva</a>. Negotiations over tariffs, debts, and issues of market access meanwhile assumed steadily greater importance. Efforts to liberalize the terms of private commerce came to involve foreign ministries, ministries of trade, and specialized ambassadors-at-large as well as resident ambassadors and consular officers.</p><!--[P94]--><p>The end of the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Cold-War" class="md-crosslink">Cold War</a> left the foreign relations of many countries without a clear direction. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Russia" class="md-crosslink">Russia</a> struggled to come to terms with its diminished power and influence, brought about by the political and economic collapse of the former Soviet Union. Deprived of its Soviet enemy and unchallenged as a global power, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink">United States</a> clung to its alliances but deferred less to its allies and found itself increasingly isolated in international forums. Europe progressed toward greater unity without developing a clear vision of its preferred place in the world, including its relationship with its long-standing American ally. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Japan" class="md-crosslink">Japan</a> more openly aspired to becoming a “normal nation,” casting off the restrictions on its international role that its defeat in <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/World-War-II" class="md-crosslink">World War II</a> had imposed, but it did little to define or realize this ambition. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/China" class="md-crosslink">China</a> and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/India" class="md-crosslink">India</a>, which had seen themselves first as victims of European aggression and then as part of a “Third World” between the American and Russian-led blocs, began uneasily to emerge as great powers in their own right, in the process reviving elements of their long-forgotten ancient diplomatic doctrines and traditions.</p><!--[P95]--><p>The world map itself changed constantly. As the Soviet Union broke up, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Baltic-states" class="md-crosslink autoxref">Baltic states</a> resumed their independence, and another wave of new states emerged from the retreat of Russian imperialism from Eastern Europe, the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Caucasus" class="md-crosslink">Caucasus</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/history-of-Central-Asia" class="md-crosslink">Central Asia</a>. Meanwhile, various multiethnic states (e.g., Yugoslavia) were torn asunder by rampant <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/nationalism" class="md-crosslink">nationalism</a>. Ethnic minorities, such as Eritreans, achieved self-determination, and civil strife in countries such as <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Afghanistan" class="md-crosslink">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Rwanda" class="md-crosslink">Rwanda</a>, and <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Somalia" class="md-crosslink">Somalia</a> resulted in great suffering and huge <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/refugee" class="md-crosslink">refugee</a> flows. The need for international intervention to assist the peoples of failed states seemed to increase constantly. In the course of efforts to assign culpability for large-scale human suffering, the walls of sovereign immunity began to be breached. Even current and former heads of state were no longer exempt from the legal process in international and national courts. At the beginning of the 21st century, there was a <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/consensus" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="consensus">consensus</a> that a transition in the diplomatic order was occurring, though there was disagreement about what kind of new order would emerge.</p><!--[P96]--></section></section></section><nav class="prev-next-pagination margin-top-50 You can make it easier for us to review and, hopefully, publish your contribution by keeping a few points in mind.        </p>        <ol>            <li>Encyclop&aelig;dia Britannica articles are written in a neutral objective tone for a general audience.</li>            <li>You may find it helpful to search within the site to see how similar or related subjects are covered.</li>            <li>Any text you add should be original, not copied from other sources.</li>            <li>At the bottom of the article, feel free to list any sources that support your changes, so that we can fully understand their context. (Internet URLs are the best.)</li>        </ol>        <p>            Your contribution may be further edited by our staff, and its publication is subject to our final approval. Unfortunately, our editorial approach may not be able to accommodate all contributions.        </p>    </div></div><div id="md-edit-leave" class="md-edit-mode-modal ui-hidden">    <div class="md-modal-header">        <div class="md-modal-title md-heading-3"></div>    </div>    <div class="md-modal-body text-center">        <div class="md-modal-message"></div>        <div class="text-center">            <button class="md-leave md-button md-link"></button>            <button class="md-submit md-button md-primary">Submit</button>        </div>    </div></div><div id="md-edit-confirmation" class="md-edit-mode-modal ui-hidden">    <div class="md-modal-header">        <div class="md-modal-title md-heading-3">Thank You for Your Contribution!</div>    </div>    <div class="md-modal-body">        <p>Our editors will review what you've submitted, and if it meets our criteria, we'll add it to the article.</p>        <p>Please note that our editors may make some formatting changes or correct spelling or grammatical errors, and may also contact you if any clarifications are needed.</p>        <div class="text-center">            <button class="md-button md-primary"></button>        </div>    </div></div><div id="md-edit-error" class="md-edit-mode-modal ui-hidden">    <div class="md-modal-header">        <div class="md-modal-title md-heading-3">Uh Oh</div>    </div>    <div class="md-modal-body text-center">        <p>There was a problem with your submission.
